~Determiners Teacher: Today, we’ll review determiners. These are words that modify or limit nouns. There are seven main categories. Let’s go over them quickly. Student: Okay, I’m ready! Teacher: First, quantifiers. These show amounts. For example, "much," "many," "several." They describe how much or how many of something. Can you give an example? Student: "Many books." Teacher: Perfect! Now, possessives. These show ownership. Example Student: "my," "your," "their." Can you try one? Student: "Her pen." Teacher: Great! Next, demonstratives. These point to something specific: "this," "that," "these," "those." Example? Student: "This chair." Teacher: Exactly. Now, articles. These identify or introduce noun Student: "a," "an," "the." Example? Student: "A dog." Teacher: Correct. Now, distributives. These refer to individuals in a group: "each," "every," "either," "neither." Example? Student: "Each person." Teacher: Great. Interrogatives are used for questions about noun Student: "which," "what," "whose." Example? Student: "Whose book?" Teacher: Good! Finally, numbers. These express specific order or quantity: "first," "second," "three." Example? Student: "She finished first." Teacher: Excellent! Remember: quantifiers for amounts, possessives for ownership, demonstratives for location, articles to introduce nouns, distributives for groups, interrogatives for questions, and numbers for order. Student: Got it! Thanks for the quick review! Teacher: You’re ready for the quiz!~ Concept Quiz ?Which category is used to show the quantity or amount of something, like in "much," "many," or "several"? Nouns are words that name things. They’re divided into four main groups. Let’s go through them. Student: Sounds good! Teacher: First, we have People. These are nouns for living beings like humans. For example, "boy," "teacher," or "doctor." Can you give one? Student: "Football player." Teacher: Exactly! Next, we have Animals. These refer to non-human living beings like "dog," "cat," or "tiger." Can you think of one? Student: "Lion." Teacher: Perfect. Now, let’s talk about Objects. These are things we can touch, like "computer," "phone," or "desk." Example? Student: "Table." Teacher: Right! Finally, Places. These are locations or geographical areas like "Paris," "Thailand," or "beach." Can you give an example? Student: "Library." Teacher: Great! Let’s break it down. People = humans, Animals = non-human living beings, Objects = things you can touch, and Places = locations. Simple, right? Student: Yes, that’s clear now! Teacher: Let’s test this. I’ll say a word, and you tell me the category. Ready? Student: Ready! Teacher: "Bangkok." Student: Places. Teacher: Correct. "Football player." Student: People. Teacher: Well done. "Dog." Student: Animals. Teacher: Great. Last one: "Computer." Student: Objects. Teacher: Perfect! You’ve got it. This will help with your quiz. Remember: People, Animals, Objects, and Places. Student: Thanks, this was easy to follow!~ Concept Quiz ?Which category includes nouns that refer to living beings like humans and animals? These pronouns replace the subject of a sentence. There are three main group Student: 1st person, 2nd person, and 3rd person. They can be singular or plural. Let’s go through them. Student: Okay, I’m ready! Teacher: First, 1st Person pronouns. These are for the speaker. For singular, we use "I." For plural, we use "we." Example? Student: "I am a student." Teacher: Good! Now, 2nd Person pronouns. These are for the person you're speaking to. Both singular and plural use "you." Example? Student: "You are my teacher." Teacher: Exactly! Now, 3rd Person pronouns. For singular, we use "he," "she," or "it." For plural, we use "they." Example? Student: "He is my friend." And, "They are teachers." Teacher: Perfect! Let’s test this. I’ll say some pronouns, and you tell me which group they belong to. Ready? Student: Ready! Teacher: "I." Student: 1st Person Singular. Teacher: Correct. "You." Student: 2nd Person Singular and Plural. Teacher: Right. "He." Student: 3rd Person Singular. Teacher: Good. "We." Student: 1st Person Plural. Teacher: Great! Now, let’s go over the groups and their examples: 1st Person Singular = "I" 1st Person Plural = "We" 2nd Person Singular and Plural = "You" 3rd Person Singular = "He," "She," "It" 3rd Person Plural = "They" Student: Okay, I think I got it! Teacher: Good. Now, let’s do a quick matching activity. I'll give you some pronouns, and you tell me which group they belong to. Teacher: "I, We." Student: 1st Person Singular and Plural. Teacher: Correct! "You, You." Student: 2nd Person Singular and Plural. Teacher: Nice. "He, She, It." Student: 3rd Person Singular. Teacher: Great! One more: "We, They." Student: 1st Person Plural and 3rd Person Plural. Teacher: Excellent! That’s the basic structure. You’re ready for the quiz now! Student: Thanks! I |You| We He ?Sarah and I are classmates. ___ like to study together. |We| They She He ~Object Pronouns Teacher: Today, we’re going to focus on object pronouns. These are pronouns that replace the object in a sentence. For example, instead of saying "I see the car," we can say "I see it." Student: Ah, so object pronouns replace the object of the verb. Teacher: Exactly! Let’s break them down into their groups. For singular, we have: 1st person singular: "me" – for example, "She called me." 2nd person singular: "you" – for example, "I see you." 3rd person singular: "him, her, it" – for example, "I see him" or "I see it." Student: So, me is for I, you is for you, and then him, her, it are for other people or things? Teacher: That’s right! Now, for plural, we have: 1st person plural: "us" – for example, "They see us." 2nd person plural: "you" – for example, "I see you all." 3rd person plural: "them" – for example, "I see them." Student: So, you can be both singular and plural? Teacher: Yes! "You" is used for both singular and plural. Now, let's practice with a few examples. I’ll give you the pronoun, and you tell me which group it belongs to. Ready? Student: Ready! Teacher: "Me." Student: 1st person singular. Teacher: Correct! "You." Student: 2nd person singular and plural. Teacher: Right. "Him." Student: 3rd person singular. Teacher: Nice! "Us." Student: 1st person plural. Teacher: Great job! Now let’s do some matching. I'll say a list of pronouns, and you tell me which group they belong to. Teacher: "Me, Us." Student: 1st person singular and plural. Teacher: Excellent! "You, You." Student: 2nd person singular and plural. Teacher: Good. "Him, Her, It." Student: 3rd person singular. Teacher: Right. "We, They." Student: 1st person plural and 3rd person plural. Teacher: Perfect! You’re doing great. To wrap up, remember: Singular object pronoun Student: me, you, him, her, it Plural object pronoun Student: us, you, them Student: Got it! Thanks for explaining, it’s clear now.~ Concept Quiz ?Which group of object pronouns refers to one person (singular)? |1st Person| 2nd Person 3rd Person Plural ?Which group of object pronouns refers to more than one person (plural)? 1st Person 2nd Person 3rd Person |Plural| ?Which object pronouns are used for 1st person singular? |Me| You Him, Her, It Us ?Which object pronouns are used for 2nd person singular? |You| Me Him, Her Us ?Which group includes "me" as an object pronoun? |1st Person Singular| 2nd Person Singular 3rd Person Singular 1st Person Plural ?Which of the following are 3rd person singular object pronouns? I want to tell ___ about it. him her |them| us ?Our teacher always listens to ___ when we have questions. |us| them me him ~Indefinite Pronouns Teacher: Alright, let’s talk about indefinite pronouns today. These pronouns are used when we don’t know exactly who or what we are talking about. For example, instead of saying "I saw John," we can say "Someone saw John," because we don’t know exactly who. Student: Ah, so indefinite pronouns are like general pronouns? Teacher: Exactly! Let's go over a few key ones. First, some common indefinite pronouns are: Someone, anyone, everyone – These refer to people, but they don’t specify who exactly. Something, anything, everything – These refer to things, but they are unspecific. Nothing, nobody, no one – These refer to the absence of people or things. Student: So, we use them when we want to talk about people or things in a general way? Teacher: Yes! Now, let’s break it down with some examples. For example: Someone is at the door. We don’t know exactly who, just that it’s a person. I don’t know anything about it. This refers to an unspecified thing. Student: I see! But how do I know which indefinite pronoun to use? Teacher: Good question. Let's go through a few categories: For people, we use pronouns like someone, anyone, no one, everyone. For example: "Everyone loves music." For things, we use something, anything, nothing, everything. For example: "I need something to drink." For groups or large numbers, we use all, many, several. For example: "Many people were there." Student: So, everyone is used when referring to all people, and everything is for all things? Teacher: Exactly! And there are also singular and plural indefinite pronouns. Let’s look at the singular ones first Teacher: someone, anyone, no one, nothing. No one came to the party. (singular) - Now, for plural, we have some, several, many. Many people came to the party. (plural) - Student: Oh, so I need to remember whether the subject is singular or plural! Teacher: That’s right. Now, let’s practice a bit. I’ll say some indefinite pronouns, and you tell me if they’re singular or plural. Teacher: "Someone." Student: Singular. Teacher: "Many." Student: Plural. Teacher: Nice! Now, let’s do some matching. I’ll give you a definition, and you pick the correct indefinite pronoun. Ready? Student: Ready! Teacher: "Refers to an unspecified number of things." Student: Anything. Teacher: Good! "Refers to a group of people but doesn’t specify who." Student: Someone. Teacher: Exactly! "Refers to every person, without exception." Student: Everyone. Teacher: Great! And finally, remember this rule: when you want to ask about people or things, you can use who or what. For example: "Who wants to come?" or "What do you need?" Student: I understand now. So, indefinite pronouns are really useful when I don’t know exactly who or what I’m talking about! Teacher: Exactly. You’ve got it! Keep practicing these, and you’ll be ready for your quiz. You did great today! Student: Thanks, I feel much more confident now!~ Concept Quiz ?Which of the following is an example of an indefinite pronoun? Nobody Everyone |Someone| Anything ?___ must finish their homework before going outside. |Everyone| Nobody Someone Anything ~Verbs Teacher: Today, we’re going to talk about verbs and their different categories. Verbs describe actions, and there are many types of actions we can talk about. So, let’s break it down. Student: Sounds good! Where should we start? Teacher: Let’s begin with the first category: Basic Actions. These verbs describe simple, everyday tasks. For example, open, close, start, stop. You can say, "Please open the door" or "I need to start my homework." Student: So, basic actions are things we do all the time, like starting or stopping something? Teacher: Exactly! Now, let’s move to Movement verbs. These describe actions where something moves from one place to another. For example, walk, run, jump, swim, go, come. You could say, "I like to run in the park." Student: Ah, these are physical movements! Teacher: Right. Next, we have Sensing verbs. These describe our perceptions – things we can see, hear, feel, taste, or smell. For example, "I can hear the music" or "I see the sunset." Student: So, sensing verbs are about using our senses? Teacher: Yes! Now, let's look at Communication verbs. These verbs are used to talk about communicating with others. For example, ask, say, tell, show. You could say, "I will ask her a question" or "She will tell me the answer." Student: These are verbs about talking to people, right? Teacher: Exactly! Next is the Thinking category. These verbs refer to mental actions like understanding, remembering, and knowing. Examples include think, know, understand, and remember. You can say, "I think I understand this" or "She knows the answer." Student: Ah, so thinking verbs are about our thoughts and understanding? Teacher: Yes! Now let’s talk about Feelings. These verbs express emotions or feelings. For example, like, love, hate, feel. You can say, "I love chocolate" or "I feel happy." Student: So, feelings verbs are about how we feel inside? Teacher: That’s right! Now, let’s move on to Possession verbs. These describe ownership, like have or own. For example, "I have a car" or "She owns a house." Student: So these are verbs that show if we own something? Teacher: Exactly. And finally, we have Travel verbs. These describe actions related to traveling or transportation. Examples are drive, ride, navigate, pilot. For example, "I will drive to the airport" or "He rides his bike every day." Student: Wow, that’s a lot of categories! How do I remember them? Teacher: Don’t worry! Let’s do a quick exercise. I’ll say a verb, and you tell me which category it belongs to, okay? Student: Got it! Teacher: "Think." Student: Thinking. Teacher: "Run." Student: Movement. Teacher: "See." Student: Sensing. Teacher: "Tell." Student: Communication. Teacher: Great! Let’s try a few more. "Own." Student: Possession. Teacher: "Swim." Student: Movement. Teacher: And how about "Love"? Student: Feelings! Teacher: Perfect! You’re getting the hang of it. Now, remember, these categories help you understand what kind of action the verb is describing: physical action, communication, thinking, etc. Once you know the categories, it’s easier to use the verbs correctly. Student: Thanks, this makes sense now. I feel ready for the quiz! Teacher: You’re doing great! Just keep practicing these categories and their verbs, and you’ll do fine. These are a special kind of verb because they describe states of being or emotions, not actions. Are you ready? Student: Yes, I’m ready! Teacher: Great. So, state verbs don’t describe physical actions like running or jumping. Instead, they describe things like emotions, mental states, and possession. Student: So, they describe how we feel or think? Teacher: Exactly! For example, verbs like believe, know, like, love, and hate are all state verbs. You wouldn’t say, "I am knowing the answer" because it’s not an action, it’s a state. Student: Oh, so I would say "I know the answer," not "I am knowing it." Teacher: Right! You use state verbs in their simple form, not in continuous tenses. State verbs don’t usually describe actions that are happening right now—they describe something that is or exists. Student: So, state verbs can’t be used like regular action verbs in continuous tenses? Teacher: Correct. For example, you wouldn’t say "I am liking pizza." You would just say, "I like pizza." But there are exceptions when a state verb can show emotion, like "I’m loving this!" But that’s a bit more informal. Student: Ah, okay! So if it’s an emotion, I can use it in the continuous tense, like "I’m loving this pizza." But not for knowing or believing, right? Teacher: Yes, exactly! Now, state verbs fall into different categories. Some describe mental processes, like think, believe, know. These are cognitive state verbs. Student: So thinking verbs are all state verbs? Teacher: Yes! And then, there are emotional state verbs, like like, love, hate. These describe how you feel about something. Student: So like and love are emotional state verbs? Teacher: Yes! And another category is possession. Verbs like have or own show that something belongs to you. For example, "I have a car" or "She owns a house." Student: Oh, I see. So have is a state verb because it shows possession, not action? Teacher: That’s right. You wouldn’t say, "I am having a car." You just say, "I have a car." It’s a state, not an action. Student: Okay, so when I want to describe something I own, I just use the simple form. Got it! Teacher: Exactly! Let’s test your understanding with a few examples. I’ll say a sentence, and you tell me if it uses a state verb correctly, okay? Student: Sure! Teacher: "I know the answer." Student: That’s correct! It’s a state verb because it’s not an action, it’s knowledge. Teacher: Perfect. How about this one: "I am liking pizza." Student: That’s incorrect. It should be "I like pizza," because like is a state verb and we don’t use it in the continuous tense. Teacher: Exactly! Now let’s try a couple of more examples. "She hates the movie." Student: That’s right. Hates is an emotional state verb. Teacher: Great! And lastly, "I own a dog." Student: That’s correct too. Own is a possession state verb. Teacher: You’re doing awesome! Just remember, state verbs describe things that are true or permanent like your opinions, emotions, or possessions. And we don’t use them in continuous tenses unless we're talking about strong emotions in casual speech. Student: Got it! State verbs are tricky because they’re different from action verbs. But now I understand. Teacher: Exactly! Now, let’s go over one more thing. There are some verbs, like believe, think, and know, that are mental state verbs. Others like love, hate, and like are emotional state verbs. And then, there are possession verbs like have and own. Student: That makes sense! I think I’m ready for the quiz now. Teacher: You’re going to do great! Just remember, state verbs are about describing a state, not an action. Practice, and you’ll be fine.~ Concept Quiz ?Which of the following best describes a state verb? These are combinations of verbs and prepositions or adverbs, and they have a unique meaning. Are you ready? Student: Yes, I’m ready! Teacher: Great. So, phrasal verbs can be tricky because their meanings are not always clear from the individual words. For example, "turn up" doesn’t just mean "to turn something up." It means "to arrive" or "to appear." Student: Oh, I see! Like, "I turned up late for the meeting." Teacher: Exactly! Now, phrasal verbs can belong to different categories depending on their meaning. Let's look at some categories, okay? Student: Sure, what are the categories? Teacher: We have categories like communication, movement and direction, work and daily activities, giving and receiving, and possession and holding. I’ll give you examples of each category. Student: That sounds easy enough. Teacher: First, let's talk about phrasal verbs related to communication. These include phrases like "say hello," "put forward," and "turn down." These verbs are all used when we are talking to others, making requests, or giving opinions. Student: So, if I say, "I put forward an idea," that’s a phrasal verb related to communication? Teacher: Yes! And "turn down" is often used to mean "reject." For example, "I turned down the invitation because I was busy." Student: Ah, okay! What about "call off"? Is that also related to communication? Teacher: Yes, exactly. "Call off" means "to cancel." So, "We called off the meeting because of the storm." Student: Got it. What’s next? Teacher: Now, let’s talk about phrasal verbs related to work and daily activities. These phrasal verbs describe things we do every day, like "set up," "look after," and "break down." Student: So "set up" means to establish something, like "They set up a new business." Teacher: Exactly! And "break down" means when something stops working, like, "My car broke down on the way home." Student: Right. What about "look after"? Does it mean the same as "take care of"? Teacher: Yes, "look after" means to take care of someone or something. For example, "She looks after her little brother." Student: I think I’m getting it! What’s next? Teacher: Let’s move on to phrasal verbs related to movement and direction. These verbs are all about movement, like "pick up," "drop off," and "take off." Student: So, "pick up" means to collect something, like "I picked up my order at the store." Teacher: Yes! And "take off" is used when something leaves the ground, like a plane: "The plane took off at 3 PM." Student: And "drop off" means to leave something at a place, right? Teacher: Correct! For example, "I dropped off the package at the post office." Student: Okay, this is making sense. Teacher: Great! Now, let’s talk about phrasal verbs related to possession and holding. These include verbs like "hold on," "carry on," and "keep up." Student: So, "hold on" means to grip something or wait, right? Teacher: Exactly. "Hold on" can mean physically holding something or telling someone to wait. For example, "Hold on, I’ll be right back!" Student: And "carry on" means to continue? Teacher: Yes, "carry on" means to continue with something, like, "Let’s carry on with the meeting." Student: What about "keep up"? Teacher: "Keep up" means to maintain the same pace or level. For example, "She couldn’t keep up with the rest of the class." Student: These categories are really helpful! What’s next? Teacher: Now, let’s practice the meanings of some phrasal verbs. I’ll give you some sentences, and you tell me the meaning of the phrasal verb. Ready? Student: Yes! Teacher: "I put off the meeting until next week." What does "put off" mean? Student: It means to delay something. Teacher: Correct! How about thi Student: "He turned down the job offer." Student: That means he rejected it. Teacher: Exactly! And what abou Teacher: "She broke up with her boyfriend." Student: That means she ended the relationship. Teacher: Right! Now, let’s try this one: "They looked after their grandparents." Student: That means they took care of them. Teacher: Perfect! Last one: "I ran into an old friend at the store." Student: That means you met them unexpectedly. Teacher: Exactly! You’re doing great. Just remember, phrasal verbs are made up of a verb and a preposition or adverb, and their meanings can vary depending on the context. They can fall into categories like communication, work and daily activities, and movement. Student: I understand now! Thanks for explaining it so clearly. I feel ready for the quiz! Teacher: I’m sure you’ll do great! Keep practicing these phrasal verbs, and you’ll master them in no time.~ Concept Quiz ?Which category includes "say hello/goodbye, put off, turn up, cut off"? on| break up turn off put off ~Phrasal Verbs B Teacher: Alright, let’s dive into phrasal verbs today. Phrasal verbs are combinations of verbs and prepositions or adverbs. They have specific meanings that you can’t always guess from the individual words. Ready? Student: Yes, I'm excited! How do we start? Teacher: Great! First, let’s break phrasal verbs into different categories. We’ll start with communication. These phrasal verbs are used when talking to others. For example, "say hello," "put forward," and "get through to." Student: So, "say hello" is a phrasal verb for greeting people? Teacher: Exactly! And "put forward" means to suggest something, like, "I put forward my idea during the meeting." "Get through to" means to make contact with someone, like, "I couldn’t get through to him on the phone." Student: Okay, I think I got it! What’s next? Teacher: Next, let’s look at phrasal verbs related to work and daily activities. These are verbs that describe things we do every day or at work. For example, "set up," "call off," and "look for." Student: So, "set up" means to arrange something, like, "I set up the meeting." Teacher: Yes, exactly! And "call off" means to cancel something. For example, "We had to call off the event because of the rain." "Look for" means to search for something, like "I’m looking for my keys." Student: Got it! What about the next group? Teacher: Now, let's talk about phrasal verbs related to movement and transport. This includes verbs like "pick up," "drop off," and "take off." Student: "Pick up" means to collect something, right? Like, "I picked up my package." Teacher: Yes! And "drop off" means to leave something or someone at a specific place. Like, "I dropped off my friend at the station." "Take off" is used for things like planes when they leave the ground, like "The plane took off at noon." Student: Great! These are easy to understand. Teacher: Good! Now, let’s move to phrasal verbs related to possession and holding. These are things like "hold on," "carry on," and "keep up with." Student: "Hold on" means to wait or hold something, right? Like, "Hold on a second, I’ll be right there!" Teacher: Exactly! And "carry on" means to continue, like, "Please carry on with your story." "Keep up with" means to stay at the same level as someone, like "I can't keep up with the pace of the class." Student: Okay, I think I understand all the categories now. But how do I know which category a phrasal verb belongs to? Teacher: Great question! Let's practice by matching phrasal verbs to categories. I’ll give you a list, and you tell me where they belong. Ready? Student: Yes, let’s do it! Teacher: Here are some phrasal verb Student: "say hello," "put off," "turn up," "get through to." Which category do these belong to? Student: These are all related to communication. Teacher: Correct! Now, here’s another se Teacher: "set up," "break down," "look for," "call off," "get along." What do you think? Student: These seem to be related to daily activities and work. Teacher: Yes! Well done. Now, let's try this one: "drop off," "pick up," "give away," "take back," "give up." Student: These belong to the giving and receiving category. Teacher: Correct again! How abou Teacher: "hold on," "carry out," "keep up with," "hold up," and "carry on"? Student: Those must be related to work and daily activities, since they’re all about continuing or completing things. Teacher: Excellent! Now, let’s take a look at these: "go out," "go over," "come up with," "go through," "come across." Student: These are all related to communication, because they often refer to talking, discussing, or meeting people. Teacher: Spot on! You’re getting it. Now, let’s go a bit deeper. I’ll give you groups of phrasal verbs. Which group describes interactions with others? Student: It’s the first group: "say hello," "put forward," "get through to," "put up with," "turn down." Teacher: Right! And if you want to describe movement or transportation, which group do you use? Student: The group with "go out," "take off," "run into," "take out," and "bring in." Teacher: Exactly! Now, if you want to talk about daily activities or work tasks, which group fits? Student: It’s the group with "look after," "check out," "set up," "look into," and "call off." Teacher: Great! Last one. If you need to talk about problem-solving or change, which group would you use? Student: It’s the one with "build up," "come up with," "make out," "bring back," and "break down." Teacher: Fantastic! You’re doing great. Now, let’s look at real-life contexts. Which phrasal verbs would you use when you need to delay or cancel something? Student: I would use "put off," "call off," and "break up." Teacher: That’s right! Now, if you want to search for something or investigate, which group would you use? Student: I’d use "look for," "look into," "check out." Teacher: Correct! And if you want to meet someone unexpectedly, like at a store, which phrasal verbs would you use? Student: I’d say "run into" and "come across." Teacher: Perfect! Now, if you're talking about experiences, actions, or situations, which group fits? Student: It’s "come up with," "go through," "break down," and "make up." Teacher: Excellent job! You’ve learned a lot today. Keep practicing these, and you’ll be ready for the quiz. Today we’re focusing on linking verbs. These verbs connect the subject of a sentence to a subject complement—a word or phrase that gives more information about the subject. Ready to start? Student: Sounds interesting! Yes, I’m ready. Teacher: Great! Let’s first talk about some basic linking verbs. These include forms of "be"—like "am," "is," "are," "was," and "were," as well as verbs like sound, look, smell, taste, feel, seem, appear, become, get, and act. Student: So, linking verbs don't show action—they connect the subject to something else? Teacher: Exactly! For example, in the sentence "The pizza smells good," "smells" connects "pizza" to the complement "good". It tells us more about the pizza. Student: Ah, so linking verbs can also be sense verbs like "smell" or "look"? Teacher: Yes! Verbs like "sound," "look," and "feel" can also be linking verbs. For instance, "The pizza looks delicious" or "I feel tired." These verbs help describe the state of being, not an action. Student: I see. But what about linking verbs with "like"? I’ve seen phrases like "look like" or "taste like." Teacher: Great observation! These are called linking verbs + "like." For example, "The pizza tastes like cheese." This shows a comparison between the pizza and cheese. It’s not describing the pizza’s state, but rather, it’s saying the pizza resembles something else. Student: So, "taste like" and "feel like" are used when we compare things? Teacher: Yes, exactly! It’s how we compare the subject to something else. But remember, not every sense verb can be used with "like." For example, you wouldn’t say "I feel like tired." You’d just say "I feel tired." Student: Got it! What’s the difference between linking verbs with "like" and just normal linking verbs? Teacher: Good question. Normal linking verbs connect the subject to a subject complement. For instance, in "The pizza smells good," "smells" links pizza to good, describing the state of the pizza. Student: So, linking verbs + "like" are for comparisons, and other linking verbs describe states? Teacher: Exactly! And now, let's practice. Look at these sentence Student: "The pizza smells good," "I feel good," "It seems like nothing goes as planned." Can you identify what kind of linking verb is being used? Student: "The pizza smells good" is a normal linking verb. "I feel good" is also a normal linking verb. But "It seems like nothing goes as planned" uses a linking verb with "like." Teacher: Perfect! You’ve got it! Now, let’s look at some specific categories. Firs Teacher: linking verbs + "like." We have "look like," "sound like," and "feel like." These all compare something to something else. For example, "That music sounds like Mozart" or "She acts like a child." Student: So, "acts like" is another comparison, not just describing her actions? Teacher: Exactly! Now, let’s talk about linking verbs with subject complements. These are sentences like "She looks happy" or "The pizza tastes delicious." They describe the subject’s state, using linking verbs like look, feel, smell, taste. Student: Got it! Now, if I say "The pizza looks good," "good" is the subject complement, right? Teacher: Yes! It's describing the pizza. Now, let’s look at the sentence: "The pizza tastes delicious." What’s the subject complement here? Student: "Delicious" is the subject complement, describing the pizza’s taste. Teacher: Great! Now, let’s do a quick review. Which sentence uses a linking verb to connect the subject to a subject complement? The pizza smells good. He eats pizza every Friday. Student: The first one, "The pizza smells good," is a linking verb because it connects "pizza" to "good." Teacher: Correct! Next, which sentence uses a linking verb to describe a state of being? He smells the pizza. The pizza tastes delicious. Student: The second one, "The pizza tastes delicious," because "tastes" is describing the state of the pizza. Teacher: Exactly! Now, let's check thi Student: Which sentence is an example of a linking verb + "like" construction? The pizza tastes like cheese. The pizza smells good. Student: The first one! "The pizza tastes like cheese" shows a comparison. Teacher: Right! And now, let’s move on to real-life contexts. Which sentence describes something similar to something else? The pizza tastes like chicken. The pizza smells good. Student: The first one, "The pizza tastes like chicken," because it’s comparing the taste of pizza to chicken. Teacher: Perfect! You’re getting the hang of this. Remember, linking verbs either describe states of being, connect to a subject complement, or help make comparisons when used with "like." Student: Thanks! I feel much more confident with linking verbs now. Teacher: Great job! Keep practicing, and you’ll be ready for the quiz. You've learned a lot today.~ Concept Quiz ?Which category do these linking verbs belong to? be, am, is, are, was, were, sound, look, smell, taste, feel, seem, appear, become, get, act Forms of "Be" Sense Verbs |Other Linking Verbs| ?Which category do these linking verbs belong to? be like, sound like, look like, taste like, feel like Sense Verbs Forms of "Be" |Linking Verbs + "Like"| ?Which category do these phrases belong to? Today we’re focusing on adjectives—words that describe or modify a noun. We’ll also learn about the correct order for using multiple adjectives in a sentence. Ready? Student: Yes, I’m ready! Teacher: Great! Let’s start by looking at types of adjectives. We can describe a noun in many ways. For example, adjectives can describe: Size: Like "big" or "small." Color: Like "red" or "blue." Condition: Like "old" or "dirty." Origin: Like "American" or "Japanese." Material: Like "wooden" or "plastic." Type or Purpose: Like "racing" or "plastic." Student: So, adjectives tell us more about a noun, right? Teacher: Exactly! For example, in the sentence "A big red ball," "big" tells us the size, and "red" tells us the color. Student: That makes sense! What if I have more than one adjective? Teacher: Good question! There’s a specific order we follow when using multiple adjectives. The general order is: Opinion (like "beautiful" or "friendly") - Size (like "big" or "small") - Age (like "old" or "new") - Color (like "red" or "blue") - Material (like "wooden" or "metal") - Origin (like "Japanese" or "Italian") - Student: So the sentence "A big red new Japanese racing car" is correct? Teacher: Exactly! That’s the right order. Big (size), red (color), new (age), Japanese (origin), and racing (purpose). Student: What if I mixed them up? Like saying "A red big new racing Japanese car"? Teacher: That’s not quite right. Remember, size comes before color, and age comes before origin. So, "A red big new Japanese racing car" would be wrong because the order is off. Student: Got it! So, what’s the correct order for "A French wooden old big clean table"? Teacher: That would be "A big old clean French wooden table". Notice how we put "big" (size) before "old" (age), and "clean" (condition) before "French" (origin). Student: I see! What about this sentence: "A big green new plastic American bottle"? Teacher: Hmm, let’s think. The order should be "A big new green American plastic bottle". So, big (size) comes before new (age), and green (color) comes before American (origin). Plastic (material) comes last. Student: This is a little tricky, but I’m getting it. How about "A wooden old small French table"? Teacher: The correct order would be "A small old wooden French table." So, small (size) before old (age), and wooden (material) before French (origin). Student: Got it! So, I just need to remember the order: Opinion, Size, Age, Color, Material, Origin. Teacher: Exactly! Let’s practice with a few more examples. How would you describe a large, new, yellow, plastic, French table? Student: That would be "A large new yellow plastic French table." Teacher: Perfect! What about this one: "A big old red wooden French chair"? Student: "A big old red wooden French chair" is already in the right order, right? Teacher: Yes! You’ve got it! Student: What about "A clean new big yellow Japanese metal chair"? Teacher: The correct order would be "A big new clean yellow Japanese metal chair." Remember, size comes before age, and age comes before condition. Student: This is starting to make more sense now. Thanks for explaining the order of adjectives! Teacher: You’re doing great! Let’s quickly review: The role of adjectives is to describe nouns, and when using more than one, remember the order: Opinion, Size, Age, Color, Material, Origin. Student: I feel ready for the quiz now! Teacher: Excellent! Keep practicing, and you’ll be a pro with adjectives in no time.~ Concept Quiz ?Which of the following adjectives describes size? |tall| friendly old wooden ?Which of the following adjectives describes color? clean |red| big French ?Which of the following adjectives describes condition? ancient |dirty| plastic slim ?Which of the following adjectives describes origin? |American| wet good green ?Which of the following adjectives describes type/purpose? |racing| thick young fat ?Which of the following adjectives describes material? |wooden| Japanese blue short ?What is the correct order of adjectives in the sentence? Today we're diving into intensifiers. These are words that add emphasis to adjectives or adverbs. Do you know what an intensifier is? Student: I think so. Is it a word that makes something stronger? Teacher: Exactly! An intensifier makes an adjective or adverb more intense or stronger. For example, "really" makes "good" stronger. So, "really good" is stronger than just "good." Student: So, intensifiers are words that add emphasis. Can you give me some examples? Teacher: Sure! There are different levels of intensifiers. Let’s look at them: Weak intensifier Student: These add a small increase in intensity. For example, "somewhat", "rather", and "quite" are weak intensifiers. Moderate intensifier Student: These add a noticeable intensity. Examples include "pretty", "really", and "very". Strong intensifier Student: These show high intensity. Words like "extremely", "totally", and "absolutely" are strong intensifiers. Extreme intensifier Student: These go to the highest possible intensity. Examples include "completely", "totally", and "absolutely". Student: So, "somewhat" is weaker than "absolutely," right? Teacher: Exactly! "Somewhat" adds a slight increase in intensity, while "absolutely" adds the highest intensity. It’s like small versus huge! Student: Got it! Can you give me some more examples? Teacher: Sure! Here’s a breakdown: Weak intensifier Student: "Somewhat," "rather," "quite". These aren’t very strong but still emphasize something. For example, "The movie was somewhat boring." This means it was a little boring, but not too much. Moderate intensifier Student: "Pretty," "really," "very". These are more common. For example, "The movie was really exciting!" This shows a higher level of excitement. Strong intensifier Student: "Extremely," "totally," "absolutely". These really add a lot of emphasis. For example, "The movie was extremely exciting!" This shows the excitement was very high. Extreme intensifier Student: These words show the highest level. Examples are "completely," "totally," and "absolutely." For instance, "The hike was absolutely exhausting." This means the hike was extremely tiring. Student: Ah, I see! So what’s the difference between moderate and strong? Teacher: Great question! Moderate intensifiers like "really" or "pretty" show noticeable intensity but not the highest possible level. Strong intensifiers, like "extremely" or "totally", show high intensity, but they’re not as extreme as "absolutely." Student: Okay, I think I understand. Can we do a quick practice? Teacher: Of course! I’ll give you some sentences, and you tell me the intensity level. Teacher: First, "The movie was somewhat interesting." What do you think? Student: Hmm, that’s weak. It means the movie was a little interesting, not very exciting. Teacher: Correct! Now, "My vacation was fairly good." Student: That’s also weak, right? It’s not saying the vacation was amazing, just a little bit good. Teacher: Exactly. How about this one: "The concert was really loud." Student: That’s moderate, right? It’s louder than just "loud", but not extreme. Teacher: Right! Now, "The hike was absolutely exhausting." Student: That’s extreme! It means the hike was very tiring. Teacher: Perfect! Now let’s check the following intensifiers. "Quite", "somewhat", "very"… which one is the weakest? Student: "Somewhat" is the weakest, right? Teacher: Yes! And "totally" and "absolutely" are the strongest. Good job. Student: Thanks! This is clearer now. I think I’m ready for the quiz! Teacher: You’re doing great! Remember, weak intensifiers show a small change, moderate ones show a noticeable increase, and strong or extreme intensifiers show high or maximum intensity.~ Concept Quiz ?What is an intensifier? |A word that adds emphasis to an adjective or adverb| A word that weakens an adjective or adverb A word that changes the meaning of the adjective or adverb A word that modifies a noun ?Which of the following are examples of weak intensifiers? somewhat, rather, quite| extremely, absolutely, totally very, completely, totally intensely, strongly, extremely ?Which of the following are examples of moderate intensifiers? |quite, really, very| somewhat, pretty, rather extremely, totally, absolutely intensely, strongly, extremely ?Which of the following are strong intensifiers? |extremely, totally, absolutely| somewhat, fairly, pretty very, really, quite rather, pretty, quite ?Which of the following are extreme intensifiers? |extremely, totally, absolutely| somewhat, fairly, pretty very, really, quite rather, pretty, quite ?What do weak intensifiers do? |They emphasize a small increase in intensity.| They emphasize the highest possible intensity level. Weak |Moderate| Strong Extreme ?The hike was absolutely exhausting. Weak Moderate Strong |Extreme| ~Question Tags Teacher: Hi! Today we’re going to practice question tags. Do you know what a question tag is? Student: I think it’s the little question at the end of a sentence, right? Teacher: Exactly! Question tags are short questions that we add to the end of statements. They help to ask for confirmation or agreement. For example: "You like pizza, don’t you?" Student: Oh, I see! But how do we choose the correct tag? Teacher: Great question! It depends on the main sentence. Let’s break it down: If the main sentence is positive, the question tag is usually negative. For example: You like pizza, don’t you? Notice how "like pizza" is positive, so we use the negative tag "don’t you." If the main sentence is negative, the question tag is usually positive. For example: She isn’t coming, is she? Since "isn’t coming" is negative, we use the positive tag "is she." Student: So, if the sentence is positive, the tag is negative. If the sentence is negative, the tag is positive? Teacher: Exactly! The question tag follows the opposite form of the main sentence. Now let’s look at some examples. Here’s a positive sentence: "He can swim, ____?" Student: That would be "can’t he?" because it's positive, so we need a negative tag. Teacher: Perfect! Now, what about this sentence: "They will be here soon, ____?" Student: That’s "won’t they?" because the sentence is positive, and we use the negative tag. Teacher: Great! Now, what if the sentence is negative? For example: "She isn’t coming, ____?" Student: The tag is "is she?" because the sentence is negative, so we need the positive tag. Teacher: Exactly! Now let’s practice with a sentence that has a future tense. For example: "They will be here soon, ____?" Student: That’s "won’t they?" because it’s a future statement, and the tag is negative. Teacher: Excellent! But remember, in some cases, the auxiliary verb in the question tag must match the one in the main sentence. For example: "You don’t like tea, ____?" Student: The correct tag is "do you" because "don’t" uses the auxiliary verb do, so the tag also uses do. Teacher: Yes! And here’s another important poin Teacher: We can’t use a random auxiliary verb. It must match the main sentence. Let’s practice with some more examples. What abou Teacher: "They didn’t go to the party, ____?" Student: That’s "did they?" because "didn’t" is the auxiliary verb. Teacher: Right! Now, let’s correct some mistakes. What about thi Student: "He can’t swim, ____?" Student: That’s wrong! It should be "can he?" because "can’t" is negative, so we use the positive "can he." Teacher: Perfect! How about this one: "We should go now, ____?" Student: The correct tag is "shouldn’t we?" because "should" is positive, so we use the negative form in the tag. Teacher: Excellent! Now let’s review one more important thing. Question tags are used to ask for confirmation or to make sure we’re correct. For example: "The test was easy, wasn’t it?" Student: So, question tags can also be used to confirm what we think is true? Teacher: Exactly! Let’s try a few more sentences. Which question tag would you use for: "I can come to the party, ____?" Student: The correct tag is "can’t I?" because it's a positive sentence, so the tag is negative. Teacher: Exactly! And finally, for this one: "You have finished your homework, ____?" Student: The tag is "haven’t you?" because "have finished" is positive, so we need a negative tag. Teacher: Great job! Now you’re ready to practice with question tags. Remember, match the auxiliary verb and reverse the positivity/negativity of the main sentence to get the correct tag. Student: Thanks! I feel confident now. I’m ready for the quiz!~ Concept Quiz ?Which of the following is the correct question tag for this sentence: You like pizza, ____? |don’t you| isn’t it do you aren’t you ?Which of the following is the correct question tag for this sentence: She isn’t coming, ____? |is she| isn’t she does she isn’t it ?Which of the following is the correct question tag for this sentence: They will be here soon, ____? |won’t they| will they aren’t they don’t they ?Which of the following is the correct question tag for this sentence: He can swim, ____? doesn’t he |can’t he| is he isn’t he ?Which of the following is the correct question tag for this sentence: We should leave now, ____? should we should not we |shouldn’t we| do we ?If the main clause is positive, which type of tag do we use? To make a statement |To ask for confirmation or agreement| To introduce a new topic To start a new sentence ?They didn’t go to the party, ____? |did they| don’t they are they didn’t they ?You have finished your homework, ____? don’t you? |didn’t you?| have you? isn’t you? ~Cause/Effect/Reason and Purpose/Result Teacher: Hi there! Today we’ll talk about cause and effect, as well as purpose and result. Are you ready? Student: Yes, I think I understand a little bit, but I’m not sure about all the details. Can you explain the difference? Teacher: Of course! Let’s start with cause and effect. When we talk about the cause of something, we’re talking about the reason why it happens. And when we talk about the effect, we’re talking about the result of that action. For example: "I didn’t go to the park because it was raining." Student: So "because" shows the cause of my action? Teacher: Exactly! "Because" connects the cause to the effect. Now, if you want to show a result, you can use adverbs like "therefore," "hence," or "thus." For example: "It was raining, therefore I didn’t go to the park." Student: Okay, so "therefore" shows the result of something. What about the prepositions—how do they fit in? Teacher: Great question! When you want to show the cause using prepositions, you can use "because of," "due to," or "as a result of". These show the reason why something happens. For example: She was late because of the traffic. The meeting was canceled due to the weather. Student: So "because of" and "due to" both show the cause, but "due to" sounds a bit more formal? Teacher: Exactly! Now, when you want to talk about the purpose or intended result of something, we use "for". For example: "She studied hard for the exam." Student: Ah, so "for" shows purpose. But, what about "so" and "because"? I’ve heard those words used for both cause and result. Teacher: Yes, they can both show cause, but the key difference is: Because explains why something happened. So shows what happens as a result. For example: "I was tired, so I went to bed early." Here, "so" shows the result of being tired. But "I went to bed early because I was tired" shows the cause. Student: I see! And what about modal verbs? How do they fit into cause/effect? Teacher: Good question! Modal verbs like "must," "should," and "may" can be used to show the necessity, possibility, or obligation resulting from something. For example: You must study for the exam so that you pass. He might be late because of the weather. Student: So "must" shows a necessity, and "might" shows a possibility. What about "should"? Teacher: Exactly! "Should" suggests a recommendation or expected result. For example: "You should eat breakfast to feel better." Student: Ah, that makes sense! Now, what about conjunctions? I’ve seen words like "if" and "unless" used in cause/effect sentences. Teacher: Yes, conjunctions connect ideas! For example: If is used to show a conditional cause—something that happens if another condition is true. Like: "If it rains, we’ll stay home." Unless shows an exception. For example: "We’ll go to the beach unless it rains." Student: So "if" is used for conditions, and "unless" is used to show what won’t happen if a condition is true? Teacher: Exactly! And now, let’s talk about how we can express results using different words. "Therefore," "thus," and "hence" are all used to show that something happens as a consequence of something else. For example: He didn’t study, therefore he failed the test. She was hungry, thus she ate lunch. Student: And what about "so that"? I’ve seen that used with purpose. Teacher: Yes! "So that" is used to show purpose or intended result. For example: "She studied hard so that she could pass the exam." Student: Great! What if I want to show the result of something from the past? Teacher: For a result from the past, you can use "as a result of" or "because of". For example: As a result of the storm, the school was closed. The flight was delayed because of the fog. Student: Okay, so "as a result of" and "because of" are both used to show past causes, but the structure of the sentence changes a little, right? Teacher: Exactly! The preposition stays the same, but the sentence structure may change depending on the cause and effect. Student: That’s a lot to remember, but I feel more confident now. I think I can start practicing with these words and structures! Teacher: Awesome! Just remember, when showing cause, use words like "because," "due to," and "as a result of". When showing effect, try "therefore," "so," and "thus." And for purpose, use "for" or "so that." Student: Thanks so much! I think I’m ready for the quiz now! Teacher: You’re welcome! Keep practicing, and you’ll do great. Good luck!~ Concept Quiz ?Which of these prepositions expresses the cause of something? for |because of| |due to| |as a result of| ?When we want to express a purpose or intended result, which preposition would we use? because of owing to |for| due to ?Which of these adverbs is used to indicate a result or consequence of something? |therefore| |hence| |thus| |all of the above| ?Which conjunction links a cause and result, showing a direct reason for something? if |so| unless since ?What conjunction would you use to show the reason for an action, similar to "because"? |since| if unless |now that| ?Which modal verb suggests a necessity or strong obligation resulting from a situation? should |must| may might ?What’s the main difference between "so" and "because" in cause/effect relationships? |"so" shows a result, "because" shows a cause| so is used only in questions because shows a result, "so" shows a cause so shows purpose, "because" shows effect ?Which of these sentences shows a purpose relationship? Today, we’re going to talk about contrast and opposition in English. You know, how to show when two things are different or contradict each other. Student: Oh, that sounds tricky. What’s the difference between contrast and opposition? Teacher: Great question! Contrast is when two ideas or actions are different, and opposition is when they directly contradict each other. For example, "I wanted to go to the park, but it was raining." Here, "but" shows opposition—going to the park directly contradicts the rain. Student: Okay, so "but" shows opposition. But what about "however," and "although"? Teacher: "However" and "although" both show contrast, but "however" is more formal, and it’s usually used at the beginning of a sentence. "Although" is used to show contrast within the same sentence. For example: I wanted to go to the park. However, it was raining. Although it was raining, I wanted to go to the park. Student: Ah, so "however" is like starting a new sentence for contrast, and "although" works in the same sentence. What about "yet"? Teacher: Yes! "Yet" is another conjunction we use to show contrast. It’s similar to "but", but it often emphasizes surprise or a stronger contradiction. For example: "I was tired, yet I went to the gym." Student: Got it! Now, when I want to show that something happened despite another situation, what do I use? Teacher: You can use "despite" or "in spite of" for that. Both of these show contrast in the face of a situation. For example: Despite the rain, we went to the park. In spite of the rain, we went to the park. Student: Okay, so "despite" and "in spite of" show contrast, even though something negative is happening. What about "though" and "although"? Are they the same? Teacher: "Though" and "although" are very similar, but "though" is a bit more casual. "Although" is slightly more formal. For example: I went to the park, though it was raining. I went to the park, although it was raining. Student: That’s clear! Now, I’ve also heard people say "instead of". Does that show contrast too? Teacher: Yes, exactly! "Instead of" shows that one thing is replacing another. It’s not contrast in the sense of opposition, but it does show a choice between two things. For example: "I had tea instead of coffee." Student: Ah, so it's about making a choice or replacement. What about when I want to show contrast between two things happening at the same time? Teacher: Good question! For that, you can use "whereas" or "while." These are used to show two things that are happening at the same time but are different or opposite in nature. For example: I love coffee, whereas she prefers tea. I like going to the gym, while my brother prefers staying home. Student: So "whereas" and "while" show two things happening in contrast at the same time! What about modal verbs? Do they show contrast? Teacher: Yes! For example, "might still" can show a possibility that contrasts with something already stated. For example: "He’s tired, but he might still go to the party." The contrast is between his tiredness and the possibility of him going to the party. Student: Oh, I see! And what about "yet" and "still"? Do they show the same thing? Teacher: Great question! Both "yet" and "still" show contrast, but they are used differently. "Yet" emphasizes something that hasn’t happened yet, often with negatives. For example: "He hasn’t called yet." Meanwhile, "still" is used when the situation is ongoing despite something else. For example: "It’s raining, but we’re still going." Student: That’s a good distinction. And what about "on the other hand"? Does it show contrast too? Teacher: Yes! "On the other hand" is used when you’re comparing two different or opposite ideas. For example: "I like tea. On the other hand, he prefers coffee." Student: So "on the other hand" is like showing two opposite ideas. If I want to express contrast with a surprising result, what do I use? Teacher: You can use "even so" or "nonetheless". They show that something surprising happens despite a condition. For example: "It was raining, even so, we went to the park." Student: Ah, so "even so" means despite the condition, something surprising happens. What about "rather than"? Teacher: "Rather than" shows a preference for one thing instead of another. For example: "I’d like to go for a walk rather than stay inside." Student: Okay, so "rather than" is more about preference, not exactly contrast. But what about showing preference in contrast? Teacher: For preference, we use "would rather". It’s used when you prefer one option over another. For example: "I would rather stay home than go to the party." Student: That makes sense! I think I’ve got it now. There’s just one thing I’m still confused about. When do I use "despite" and "in spite of"? Are they the same? Teacher: Yes, "despite" and "in spite of" are very similar. There’s no big difference, but "despite" is more common and slightly more formal. They both show that something happened in contrast to a situation. For example: "She went to work despite being sick." or "She went to work in spite of being sick." Student: Got it! So now I understand how to use contrast and opposition in English. Thanks so much for explaining all of this! Teacher: You’re welcome! Keep practicing, and you’ll get even better at using these in conversation. Cause Result |Contradiction/contrast| Condition ~Frequency Teacher: Hey there! Today, we’re going to dive into frequency—how often something happens in English. This is all about using adverbs, prepositions, and modal verbs to show how often an action takes place. Ready? Student: Yes, I’m ready! So, what exactly does frequency mean? Teacher: It’s about showing how often something occurs. We can use words like always, often, or rarely to describe this. For example, "I go to the gym every day," means something happens regularly, or every single day. Student: Oh, so "every day" shows regularity. But what if I want to say that something happens just once in a while? Teacher: In that case, we use "once in a while" or "occasionally." Both of these suggest that something happens from time to time, but not very often. For example: "I go to the movies once in a while." Student: So "once in a while" is like saying something happens occasionally. What if I want to describe something I do most of the time, but not always? Teacher: Then you’d use "usually" or "normally." These words mean that something happens in the usual or normal pattern, but it's not guaranteed every single time. For example: "I usually have breakfast at 7 AM." Student: And "normally" is similar to "usually". Got it! What about something that happens 100 of the time? Teacher: For that, we use "always." If something happens always, it’s guaranteed to happen every time. Like: "She always drinks coffee in the morning." Student: Ok, so "always" is for things that happen all the time. But what about if something happens 50 of the time? Teacher: For that, we use "sometimes." It’s like saying, "It happens half of the time." For example: "I sometimes go for a walk in the evening." Student: Ah, "sometimes" is for things that happen about half the time. What about if something happens rarely or infrequently? Teacher: You would use "rarely" or "seldom." Both words show that something happens very infrequently. For example: "I rarely eat junk food." Student: So "rarely" is for things that almost never happen. And if something happens only occasionally, what word should I use? Teacher: Exactly, "occasionally" is perfect for that. It means it happens, but not very often. Like: "I occasionally travel for work." Student: Got it! But if something happens almost never, would I use "never"? Teacher: Yes, if it happens never, you can simply use "never." For example: "I never watch TV." You could also use "hardly ever" for a softer version, like "I hardly ever go out on weekdays." Student: So "never" is for things that don’t happen at all, and "hardly ever" means very rarely. What about things that happen frequently, but not always? Teacher: For that, you could use "frequently" or "regularly." These words mean something happens often, but not all the time. For example: "I regularly go to yoga class." Student: "Frequently" and "regularly" both mean often, but they don’t have to happen all the time. What if I want to say something happens when a condition is met? Teacher: You can use "whenever" for that. For example: "You can play outside whenever it’s sunny." This shows that the action depends on a condition—sunny weather. Student: So "whenever" is like saying "every time" something happens, right? And how do I talk about something that could happen occasionally in the future? Teacher: For future possibilities, we can use modal verbs like "may" or "might." For example: "I might go to the beach this weekend." This shows a possibility but not certainty. Student: What about if I want to say something could happen now, but only sometimes? Teacher: You could say "can sometimes" to show that something is possible, but happens occasionally. For example: "It can sometimes rain during the summer." Student: So "can sometimes" means it’s possible, but not guaranteed. What if I want to say something that happens every week? Teacher: For something that happens every week, we use "every week". For example: "I go to the gym every week." Student: Nice! I think I’m getting it now. What about using "if" to show frequency? Teacher: Good question! We use "if" when we talk about something that happens whenever a condition is met. For example: "I will go to the park if it’s sunny." This shows that the action depends on the weather. Student: So "if" shows a condition that determines whether something will happen. Can you give me an example with a modal verb like "can"? Teacher: Sure! You could say: "I can go out whenever I finish my homework." Here, "can" shows the possibility, and "whenever" indicates the condition. Student: Got it! So now I understand how to use different frequency words like "always," "sometimes," "rarely," and "whenever" in sentences. Thanks, this was really helpful! Teacher: You’re welcome! Keep practicing, and you’ll get even better at using these in everyday conversations. Don’t forget—frequency is all about showing how often something happens. Something happens regularly. Something happens very infrequently. Something happens with certainty. |Something is a possible or occasional event.| ~Degree, Intensity, and Emphasis Teacher: Hi there! Today, we’ll talk about degree and intensity in English. This is how we describe how much or how strongly something happens. We use different adverbs to show how much or how little something is. Ready? Student: Yes, I’m ready! So, what do you mean by degree and intensity? Teacher: Great question! Degree refers to how strong or weak something is. Intensity shows how much of something there is. For example, if you say something is "very" good, it means it's quite strong. But if you say it's "slightly" good, it's not as strong. Student: Ah, I see! So, "very" means strong, and "slightly" means a little. What about when something is more than needed or too much? Teacher: If something is more than expected, we use "too". For example, "It’s too hot outside" means the temperature is more than what is comfortable. It shows excess. Student: So, "too" is for excess. What about if something is extremely intense or strong? Teacher: When something is extremely intense or high-level, we use "extremely." Like: "The movie was extremely exciting." It shows a very high degree of excitement. Student: Okay, so "extremely" is for high intensity. What if I want to show something is almost true, but not quite? Teacher: In that case, you’d use "almost." For example: "I’m almost done with my homework." It means you're very close, but not quite finished. Student: "Almost" means near a point but not exactly there. And if something is very mild or not strong, what would I use? Teacher: For something slightly strong or mild, we use "slightly." For example: "The soup is slightly salty." This means just a little bit. Student: So "slightly" is for small degrees. What if something happens a lot, but still in a reasonable way? Teacher: For that, you’d use "very." It shows a lot of something, but not to excess. Like: "I very much enjoyed the concert." It’s strong, but not overwhelming. Student: "Very" is for moderate intensity. But what if something is too much or beyond what’s needed? Teacher: If it’s more than necessary, we use "too." For example: "It’s too late to call now." This shows that it’s beyond the expected or required. Student: So, "too" means more than what’s needed. What about when I want to say something is close to happening but not fully there? Teacher: If something is nearly happening, you can say "almost." For example: "I’m almost finished with the project." It’s near completion, but not quite. Student: Okay, so "almost" shows something is close, but not exactly the same as the final point. What if I want to show something has a high degree of something, but not excessive? Teacher: For a high degree that is not excessive, you can use "very" or "extremely". For example: "The test was very difficult," or "She’s extremely talented." Student: I get it! So, "very" is for moderate intensity, and "extremely" is for the highest degree. But what if I want to show something is mildly intense? Teacher: For mild intensity, you can use "slightly." For example: "I’m slightly tired after the run." It’s a small amount of tiredness. Student: So, "slightly" means small degree. What about something that’s not quite at its full potential, but very close? Teacher: If something is almost at its maximum, you’d use "almost." For example: "The cake is almost ready." It’s not quite finished, but very close. Student: And what about when I want to say something is excessive or too much? Teacher: "Too" would be your choice! For example: "It’s too cold to go outside." This shows that it’s more than comfortable or expected. Student: So "too" means more than necessary. What about if I want to show that something happens rarely or at a small degree? Teacher: For something rare or mild, you could say "slightly." For example: "I’m slightly nervous about the presentation." Student: Got it! So, to sum up, "very" and "extremely" show strong intensity, while "slightly" shows a milder degree. "Too" means excess, and "almost" means something is nearly true but not fully there, right? Teacher: Exactly! You’ve got it. extremely very |almost| too ~Focus/Limitation Teacher: Welcome back! Today, we’re going to learn about adverbs that show focus or limitation. These adverbs help you explain how much something is limited or focused on a specific idea. Ready? Student: Yes! I’m curious about how these adverbs work. What does focus or limitation mean? Teacher: Good question! Focus means concentrating on something specific, and limitation means putting boundaries on something, like saying it only applies to a certain thing or under certain conditions. Student: Oh, okay. So, what adverb would I use to show that something is limited or focused on one thing? Teacher: To show that something is focused or exclusive, we use "only". For example: "I only have 5 minutes left." This means that the 5 minutes is all I have, no more. Student: So, "only" means limited to one thing. What about something that happens very recently or in a small amount? Teacher: For something that’s recent or just a small action, we use "just." For example: "I’ve just finished my homework." This means it happened very recently. Student: Ah, so "just" means recent or minimal. What if I want to show something is unexpected or surprising? Teacher: To show something surprising or unexpected, we use "even." For example: "She even passed the hardest exam." It’s surprising that she passed, especially because it was difficult. Student: I see! "Even" adds a sense of surprise. What if I want to add information to a statement? Teacher: To add more information, we use "also." For example: "I like coffee, and I also enjoy tea." It shows something extra. Student: So, "also" adds related information. What adverb would I use if I want to emphasize that something is the main or most important? Teacher: If you want to emphasize the most important thing, use "mainly." For example: "The book is mainly about friendship." It shows the primary focus of the book. Student: So, "mainly" tells us the primary focus. What if I want to say something happens most of the time, but not always? Teacher: To show that something happens most of the time, use "mostly." For example: "I mostly drink water during the day." It’s the general habit, but not always. Student: "Mostly" means something happens most of the time. What if I want to highlight something very specific or unique? Teacher: To highlight something special or specific, use "especially." For example: "I love fruit, especially mangoes." It shows that mangoes are my favorite. Student: So, "especially" emphasizes something specific. What if I want to express something that is exclusive or applies only to one thing? Teacher: For exclusivity, use "solely." For example: "This task is solely my responsibility." This means that I am the only one responsible for it. Student: So "solely" means only or exclusively. What if I want to say something is mostly true, but with a focus on one thing? Teacher: In that case, use "particularly." For example: "She is particularly good at math." This emphasizes her ability in math, more than other subjects. Student: "Particularly" gives a specific focus. What about when I want to say something happens under certain conditions? Teacher: To express that something happens only under specific conditions, you use "only." For example: "You can enter only if you have the ticket." This shows a limitation based on a condition. Student: Ah, so "only" puts a limit on when something can happen. What if I want to say something is expected, but still surprising? Teacher: If something is unexpected, even though it might be expected, use "even." For example: "He even completed the challenge faster than I expected." It’s a surprise. Student: "Even" shows something unexpected. What if I want to express that something happens in most cases but not always? Teacher: If something happens most of the time, you could use "mostly" or "mainly." For example: "I’m mostly free on weekends." or "She mainly works from home." Student: So "mostly" and "mainly" mean that something happens most of the time, but not always. What about when I want to say something is focused on one thing only? Teacher: If something focuses only on one thing, use "just." For example: "We are just talking about the future, not the past." Student: So "just" is for one specific thing. What about when something is important or of priority? Teacher: To emphasize importance, use "particularly." For example: "This project is particularly important for the company." It shows that the project is of special significance. Student: I get it! So "particularly" means something is of great importance. What about if something is very close to happening, but not fully there yet? Teacher: For something that’s nearly there, use "just." For example: "The meeting is just starting." It means the meeting is about to start very soon. Student: So, "just" can also mean almost happening, right? Teacher: Exactly! And now you’ve learned how to use all these adverbs to show focus and limitation. How do you feel about the quiz now? Student: I feel much more confident! Thanks for explaining all of that. Teacher: You’re welcome! |just| mainly even solely ~Certainty/Probability Teacher: Welcome back to our lesson! Today, we’re diving into adverbs and modal verbs that show certainty and probability. Ready? Student: Absolutely! I’ve always found it tricky to know how to express whether something will definitely happen or just might. Teacher: No problem! Let’s start with certainty. When something is completely certain, we use words like "definitely". For example: "She will definitely come to the party." It’s clear, with no doubt. Student: So, "definitely" means it’s 100 certain? Teacher: Exactly! If you want to express high probability or something likely to happen, you can use "probably". For example: "I will probably go to the store later." It’s very likely, but not guaranteed. Student: Oh, so "probably" means it’s more likely than not, but not absolutely certain. Teacher: Correct! Now, if something might happen but is not certain, we use "maybe" or "possibly". For example: "I’ll maybe go to the park later." It’s possible, but uncertain. Student: Ah, so "maybe" or "possibly" show a weaker likelihood than "probably". Teacher: Right. Now, let’s talk about uncertainty. If something is uncertain or there’s doubt, we use words like "maybe" or "uncertainly". For example: "Maybe he will come, but I’m not sure." Student: So "maybe" shows doubt. But what if I want to express assumed truth or something that is likely but not confirmed? Teacher: That’s when we use "presumably" or "apparently". For example: "He’s presumably at work now." We’re assuming it’s true, but we don’t know for sure. Student: "Presumably" means we assume something is true based on what we know. Teacher: Exactly! Moving on, we also have modal verbs that show probability. For example, if something is likely to happen, we use "will". For example: "She will probably come to the meeting." Student: So "will" means something is likely to happen in the future. Teacher: Yes, but if something might happen, but we’re less sure, we use "may" or "might". For example: "It may rain tomorrow." or "He might join us for dinner." These are weaker possibilities. Student: So "may" and "might" are for possible, but not definite, outcomes. Teacher: Exactly. And if we want to express necessity or something that must happen, we use "must". For example: "You must wear a helmet for safety." Student: "Must" shows something is necessary or certain. Teacher: That’s right! And if you want to show a hypothetical situation, use "would". For example: "If I had time, I would go on vacation." This shows something that could happen under the right conditions, but it’s not certain. Student: So "would" is for hypothetical situations. What about showing a condition that could lead to something? Teacher: For that, you would use "if". For example: "If it rains, we will stay inside." It shows that one event depends on another. Student: Ah, so "if" is for conditional situations. How about expressing a hypothetical condition with a lower likelihood? Teacher: For a lower likelihood, you would use "could". For example: "She could win the race, but it’s not certain." It’s a possibility, but not guaranteed. Student: So "could" means there’s a chance, but it’s not certain. What about a clear inference, like something that seems obvious? Teacher: For a clear inference, we can use "undoubtedly" or "certainly." For example: "He’s undoubtedly the best player on the team." or "She will certainly help us with the project." Student: So "undoubtedly" and "certainly" mean clear certainty—there’s no doubt. Teacher: Exactly! Now, let’s do a quick review of what we’ve learned: Definite Certainty: Use "definitely" (e.g., "She will definitely arrive on time.") - High Probability: Use "probably" (e.g., "I will probably go to the movies.") - Possible but Uncertain: Use "maybe" or "possibly" (e.g., "It might possibly rain later.") - Hypothetical/Conditional: Use "if" or "would" (e.g., "If I had money, I would travel.") - Necessity: Use "must" (e.g., "You must submit the report tomorrow.") - Weaker Possibility: Use "might" or "may" (e.g., "She might come by the office.") - Assumed Truth: Use "presumably" or "apparently" (e.g., "He’s apparently already left.") - Student: This makes sense! I feel much more confident about how to express different levels of certainty now. Teacher: I’m glad to hear that! Keep practicing, and you’ll be ready for the quiz in no time.~ Concept Quiz ?Which preposition expresses something that is certain without any doubt? |beyond doubt| without question in case unless ?Which adverb shows something is certain or undoubtedly true?
