|probably| surely certainly definitely ~Deduction Teacher: Welcome back! Today, we’re focusing on modal verbs used for deduction—which help us express certainty, probability, and possibility about the present and past. Ready? Student: Yes, I’m excited to understand how to talk about what I think or assume happened! Teacher: Great! Let's begin with present deduction. When you’re sure something is happening or is true right now based on evidence, you use "must". For example: "He must be at work now." You’re using evidence, like his car in the parking lot, to say this. Student: So, "must" means it’s certain based on evidence. Got it! What if I’m less sure but it’s still likely? Teacher: Then you can use "might" or "could". For example: "He might be in a meeting right now." or "He could be working from home." This shows possibility but not certainty. Student: Okay, so "might" and "could" are for things that are possible, but we’re not sure. Teacher: Exactly. Now, if you're certain something didn’t happen in the past, you would use "must not have". For example: "He must not have gone to the party." You’re deducing, based on what you know, that it’s not true. Student: So, "must not have" means something is impossible in the past. Teacher: Yes, that's right! Now, if you want to show past deduction about something that probably happened, you use "must have". For example: "She must have left early." You can guess this based on the evidence, like her things being gone. Student: So "must have" is a strong guess about the past. Teacher: Exactly! For things that might have happened but you're not sure, you use "might have" or "could have". For example: "He might have been there," or "She could have finished her work already." Student: So "might have" and "could have" show uncertainty about the past. Teacher: Exactly. Let’s move on to rules. If something is done under a rule, you use the preposition "under". For example: "You must follow the rules under the law." Student: So "under" shows it’s following a rule or guideline. Teacher: Right! But, if something is done without permission, you use "without". For example: "You cannot leave without permission." Student: So "without" means no permission, and "under" means following a rule. Teacher: Exactly. Now, let’s look at past obligations. If someone should have done something in the past but didn’t, you use "should have". For example: "He should have called you by now." It’s a deduction that he didn’t do what he was supposed to. Student: So "should have" shows a past obligation that wasn’t fulfilled. Teacher: Yes, very good! And if something wasn’t possible in the past, you use "couldn’t have". For example: "She couldn’t have been at the party because she was at work." This shows impossibility in the past. Student: "Couldn't have" is for something impossible in the past. What about present deduction? Teacher: For present deduction, when you're certain something is happening, you use "must". For example: "It must be raining outside." This is based on the sound of rain you hear. Student: So "must" shows certainty about what’s happening right now. Teacher: Exactly! Let’s quickly review the key points: Present Deduction (Certain): Use "must". Example: "It must be 5 o’clock." Present Deduction (Possible): Use "might" or "could". Example: "She might be studying now." Past Deduction (Certain): Use "must have". Example: "He must have gone home already." Past Deduction (Possible): Use "might have" or "could have". Example: "She could have forgotten about the meeting." Past Deduction (Impossible): Use "must not have". Example: "They must not have seen the email." Present/General Rule: Use "under" for a rule, and "without" for lack of permission. Past Obligation (Unfulfilled): Use "should have". Example: "You should have apologized." Impossibility (Past): Use "couldn't have". Example: "He couldn't have been there." Student: This is really helpful! I feel more confident about how to use these words to talk about what I think happened or will happen. Teacher: I’m glad to hear that! Keep practicing these and you’ll be ready for the quiz. Deduction is all about making logical guesses based on evidence or past knowledge.~ Concept Quiz ?Which of the following modal verbs is used to express logical certainty about the present or future? might should |must| would ?Which modal verb is used for present deduction, implying something is certain based on evidence? should |must| might could ?If you are sure something did not happen, which modal verb would you use? |must not have| might have could have would have ?Which of the following phrases indicates past deduction about something that probably happened? |must have| should have might could ?Which modal verb expresses a past deduction about something that might have happened? |might have| must have should have could ?Which preposition expresses something done under a rule? with |under| for without ?Which modal verb is used to express logical deduction based on available evidence? might can |must| should ?Which preposition indicates doing something without permission? |without| under within with ?Which of the following is a correct deduction about something that happened in the past? |He must have left early.| He must leave early. He might have finished the report. He would have finished the report. ?Which of the following modal verbs expresses permission? must |can| cannot should ~Advice/Suggestions Teacher: Welcome back! Today, we’re going to talk about giving advice, making suggestions, and how to express similarity and difference in English. Are you ready? Student: Yes, I'm ready! I always get confused between giving advice and making suggestions. Teacher: That’s a great place to start. Let’s start with modal verbs for advice. The most common one for giving advice is "should". For example: "You should take a break." This is strong advice. Student: So, "should" is for advice, like when it’s important to do something? Teacher: Exactly! If you're suggesting something that’s less urgent, you can use "could". For example: "You could take a break if you want." It's still a suggestion, but not as strong as "should." Student: So "could" sounds more polite or optional? Teacher: Right! And if you want to make a polite suggestion, you can also use "would". For example: "Would you like to take a break?" It's a way to offer something kindly. Student: So "should" is stronger advice, and "could" and "would" are more polite or less strong suggestions. Teacher: Yes, exactly! Now, when we talk about giving strong advice or recommendations, we often use "should". For example: "You should see a doctor." Student: What about something that’s just a possibility? Teacher: If it's possible, you can use "might" or "could". For example: "You might want to rest," or "You could try exercising more." Both are soft suggestions or possibilities. Student: So, "might" or "could" are used when something isn’t certain, but you're suggesting it? Teacher: Exactly! Let’s quickly review: Giving Advice: Use "should" for strong advice, "could" for polite suggestions, and "would" for offers. Student: This makes so much sense now! Teacher: Exactly! Keep practicing. You could try the new restaurant. |You should try the new restaurant.| You might try the new restaurant. You should not try the new restaurant. ~Comparison/Similarity/Difference Teacher: Now, let’s talk about comparison and similarity. If you want to show that two things are similar, you use "like". For example: "He runs like a cheetah." Student: So, "like" is for comparing things that are similar? Teacher: Yes! And if you want to show equality, you can use "as...as". For example: "She is as tall as her brother." This shows they are equal in height. Student: Oh! So "as...as" is for exact equality. Teacher: Exactly! Now, if you want to show that one thing is better or worse than another, you use "than". For example: "This book is better than that movie." You’re comparing two things that are different. Student: So "than" is for comparing things that aren’t the same. What if two things are opposite or contrast with each other? Teacher: You would use "in contrast to" or "whereas". For example: "He likes tea, whereas she prefers coffee." This shows a difference in their preferences. Student: So "whereas" is for showing contrast between two ideas? Teacher: Exactly. And when you want to say two things are similar in how they are done, use "similarly". For example: "He worked similarly to her." They worked in the same way. Student: So, "similarly" means doing something the same way? Teacher: Yes, that’s right! And if you want to show something is done differently, use "differently". For example: "They solved the problem differently." Student: So "differently" shows contrast in the way something is done? Teacher: Exactly! Now, let’s look at contrast with conjunctions. To show contrast, you use "but". For example: "She’s smart, but she’s not very confident." Student: "But" shows a contrast between two ideas. What about if I want to show comparison between two similar things? Teacher: In that case, you could use "as...as" again for equality. For example: "This task is as difficult as the last one." Student: So "as...as" is for equal comparison, and "than" is for showing difference. Teacher: Yes, exactly! Let’s quickly review comparisons and similarities: Like: Shows similarity. Example: "He is like his father." As...as: Shows equality. Example: "She is as tall as me." Than: Shows difference. Example: "She runs faster than him." Contras Teacher: Use "but", "whereas", or "in contrast to" to show a difference between two things. Student: This makes so much sense now! Teacher: You’ve got it! She runs faster than him. |She runs as fast as him.| She runs like him. She runs more fast than him. ~Manner Teacher: Hi there! Today we’re going to talk about adverbs of manner and how we describe how actions are done. Ready? Student: Yes, I’m excited! I’m a bit confused about adverbs that show how we do things. Teacher: No worries! Let’s start with some basic adverbs that describe how we do actions. For example, if someone does something fast, we can say they do it quickly. For example: "She runs quickly." Student: So quickly describes doing something in a fast way, right? Teacher: Exactly! Now, if you do something slowly, we use the adverb slowly. For example: "He speaks slowly." That’s the opposite of quickly. Student: What about when someone does something carefully, like making sure it’s done right? Teacher: Good question! When someone does something with care or attention, we use the adverb carefully. For example: "She drove carefully in the snow." It means she was cautious. Student: So, carefully is used when we do things with attention to detail, right? Teacher: Yes! Now, if someone is happy while doing something, we use happily. For example: "He danced happily." It means he was enjoying the action. Student: So happily shows that someone is enjoying what they are doing. Teacher: Exactly! Now, if someone is not doing something well, we use badly. For example: "He did the homework badly." Student: Badly means something is done in a poor way? Teacher: Right! It’s the opposite of doing something well, like "She did the test well." Student: Okay! So well is used when something is done with skill or competence? Teacher: Yes, exactly! Now, let’s talk about prepositions that show the method or agent of an action. If you’re doing something with a tool or means, we often use "with". For example: "She cut the paper with scissors." Student: So, "with" shows what tool or means was used to do something? Teacher: Exactly! If you’re talking about how something is done through a process or medium, you can use "via" or "through". For example: "I learned English via online classes." Or: "The message was sent through email." Student: Ah, so "via" and "through" are for actions done through a medium or process. Teacher: Exactly! And what if something is done without any tools or means? Student: I think we would use "without"? Like, "She worked without a computer." Teacher: Perfect! You got it. Now, what if something is done on purpose, not by accident? Student: I think it’s "on purpose", like "**He broke the vase on purpose." Teacher: Yes, exactly! It means the action was intentional. Now, if something is done in a disrespectful manner, what adverb would you use? Student: I’d say "rudely", like: "He spoke rudely to the teacher." Teacher: Right! "Rudely" describes an action done in a disrespectful or impolite manner. Student: And what if someone is speaking in a polite way? Teacher: You would use "politely", like "She answered the question politely." Student: So, "politely" is the opposite of "rudely". Teacher: Exactly! Now, let’s talk about modal verbs and how they express suggestions about how something should be done. For example, if I want to suggest something, I might say: "You should do it carefully." Student: So "should" is used to suggest how something ought to be done? Teacher: Yes! And if we’re uncertain about how something might happen, we can use "could". For example: "It could be done quickly." Student: So "could" expresses uncertainty about how something is done, but it’s still possible? Teacher: Exactly! Now, let’s wrap up with some prepositional phrases that describe how actions are done. For example: "in a friendly manner," "on purpose," or "with care." Student: So these phrases describe how something is done, like: "**He spoke in a friendly manner." Teacher: Exactly! These are all examples of how to describe manner or the way an action is completed. Student: So to summarize, we can use adverbs like quickly, carefully, rudely, and happily to describe how actions are done. And we use prepositions like with, via, and without to show the means or method. Teacher: You’ve got it! Now, keep practicing these adverbs and prepositions. |happily| angrily sadly clearly ?What preposition expresses the method or way something is done (like speaking or behaving)? |in| by on through ~Direction/Movement and Place/Location Teacher: Hi there! Today we’re going to talk about direction, movement, and location. I’ll explain the key prepositions and adverbs, and how to use them. Ready? Student: Yes! I’m curious about how to use prepositions for movement and places correctly. Teacher: Perfect! Let’s start with movement. When you’re going toward a place, you use the preposition "to". For example: "I’m going to the store." Student: So "to" shows movement toward a destination? Teacher: Exactly! Now, if something is moving away from a place, you use "from". For example: "She walked from the park." Student: So "from" means moving away from a place? Teacher: Yes, that’s right! Now, if you want to say you are heading in the direction of a place, you use "toward". For example: "He is walking toward the door." Student: "Toward" shows the direction you are going, but you haven’t reached the destination yet? Teacher: Exactly! Now, if you want to describe upward movement, the adverb "up" is used. For example: "The bird flew up into the sky." Student: So "up" means higher direction, right? Teacher: Yes! And if you’re moving back to a starting point, you would use the adverb "back". For example: "I’m going back to my home." Student: So "back" means returning to where you started? Teacher: That’s it! Now, when you describe movement from one place to another, the prepositions "from" and "to" are used. For example: "She moved from the city to the countryside." Student: "From" shows where you start, and "to" shows where you’re going? Teacher: Exactly! And if something is moving forward, meaning in the direction ahead, you use the adverb "forward". For example: "He walked forward in the line." Student: So "forward" describes movement in the ahead direction. Teacher: Yes! Now, let’s talk about location. When something is inside a place, we use the preposition "in". For example: "The book is in the bag." Student: "In" means inside a space? Teacher: Yes! And if something is on a surface, like a table or a chair, you use "on". For example: "The cup is on the table." Student: So "on" is for things resting on surfaces, right? Teacher: Exactly! Now, if something is located next to or beside something else, you use "next to". For example: "The bank is next to the supermarket." Student: "Next to" shows something is right beside another thing? Teacher: Correct! If something is near a place, but not directly next to it, we use "near". For example: "The park is near my house." Student: So "near" means close to a place but not exactly next to it? Teacher: Yes! Now, if something is in the middle of two points, you use "between". For example: "The school is between the park and the library." Student: "Between" means in the middle of two things? Teacher: Exactly! And if something is below another thing, you use "under". For example: "The shoes are under the table." Student: So "under" is used for things beneath other things? Teacher: Yes! And if something is above another thing, you use "above". For example: "The picture is above the sofa." Student: "Above" means higher than something else? Teacher: Correct! And if you want to describe something in a specific location, you use "at". For example: "I’m at the bus stop." Student: "At" is used for a specific point? Teacher: Yes, that’s right! And if you want to say something is everywhere, meaning in all locations, you use "everywhere". For example: "People were everywhere at the concert." Student: "Everywhere" means all places at once? Teacher: Exactly! And lastly, if something is located somewhere, but not exactly in a specific place, you can use "there". For example: "I left my keys there." Student: So "there" is used for places that are not specific, but you know where they are? Teacher: Yes! Now, let’s review. When you describe movement: To means toward a destination. From means away from a place. Toward means heading in the direction of something. Up describes moving higher. Back means returning to a starting point. Forward means moving ahead. When talking about location: In means inside a place. On means on a surface. Near means close to, but not necessarily next to. Next to means beside. Between means in the middle of two things. Under means below. Above means higher than. At indicates a specific location. There means a place that is not specific. Everywhere means in all locations. Student: Wow, I think I understand it all now! This makes more sense. Thank you! Teacher: You did great! Just keep practicing, and you’ll have no problem with these in your quiz.~ Concept Quiz ?Which preposition indicates movement toward a destination? |to| from away from toward ?If something is moving away from a place, which preposition would you use? |in| on under next to ?Which preposition would you use to describe movement from a location? |from| to near between ~Adding Information and Ideas to Sentences Teacher: Hello! Today, we’ll be learning how to add information and ideas to sentences. We’ll look at conjunctions, prepositions, and adverbs that help you link your ideas clearly. Are you ready? Student: Yes, I am! I want to learn how to make my sentences sound more connected. Teacher: Great! Let's start with conjunctions. A conjunction links two ideas in a sentence. For example, when you want to add a new idea, you use "and". For instance: "I like reading, and I enjoy writing." Student: So "and" connects two ideas that are related? Teacher: Exactly! Now, if you want to show contrast or something different, you use "but". For example: "I wanted to go outside, but it started raining." Student: "But" shows that the second idea contrasts with the first one? Teacher: Yes! Another way to add an idea is by using "both...and". For example: "She speaks both English and French." Student: So "both...and" shows two related ideas, right? Teacher: Exactly! Another useful phrase for adding related ideas is "not only...but also". This adds emphasis. For example: "He is not only smart but also kind." Student: "Not only...but also" makes the second idea even more important, right? Teacher: Yes! It adds emphasis to show both ideas are significant. Now, let's talk about when you want to show cause and effect. You can use "because" to explain why something happens. For example: "She was late because the bus was delayed." Student: So "because" explains why something happens? Teacher: Yes! And if you want to show a reason for an action, you can use "so" to connect the ideas. For example: "I was tired, so I went to bed early." Student: "So" shows results of the first idea? Teacher: Exactly! Now, let’s look at some words used to add more information. You can use "in addition" to introduce more ideas. For example: "In addition, she also loves painting." Student: "In addition" means the same as saying "also"? Teacher: Yes, exactly! You could also use "furthermore" to add information. For example: "Furthermore, he enjoys playing the guitar." Student: "Furthermore" sounds more formal, right? Teacher: Yes, it’s often used in more formal writing or speech. Another word is "besides", which also adds information. For example: "Besides, we need to plan for the trip." Student: "Besides" adds extra information about something, right? Teacher: Yes, exactly! Now, let’s talk about time and place. If you want to introduce a dependent clause referring to time, you use "when". For example: "I remember the day when we met." Student: So "when" refers to a time? Teacher: Yes! For place, you use "where". For example: "This is the park where we play football." Student: "Where" tells us about the place, right? Teacher: Correct! If you want to explain why something happens, you can use "why". For example: "She explained why she was late." Student: So "why" explains the reason? Teacher: Exactly! Now, if you want to emphasize or reinforce a point, you can use the adverb "also". For example: "I enjoy cooking, and I also like baking." Student: "Also" adds extra information? Teacher: Yes! Another word for emphasis is "too". For example: "I can help you, too." Student: "Too" means the same as "also"? Teacher: Yes! You can use both in similar ways. Now, let's talk about "however". It’s used to contrast ideas. For example: "I wanted to go swimming; however, it started raining." Student: "However" is used to show contrast, right? Teacher: Yes, that’s right! Now, to add extra information, you can use the phrase "what's more". For example: "What’s more, we have to finish this project today." Student: "What’s more" means "in addition", right? Teacher: Exactly! Now, let’s review some other useful ways to add ideas: Not only...but also: Adds emphasis. In addition: Adds extra information. Furthermore: Also adds information, often in a formal way. Besides: Adds more detail to what you’ve already said. Also: Adds extra information or reinforces an idea. Too: Another way to say "also." Student: This is really helpful! So, I just need to practice using these words to connect my ideas better. Teacher: Yes, exactly! The more you practice, the easier it will get. You're doing great!~ Concept Quiz ?Which word introduces a dependent clause that refers to time? where why |when| how ?What word refers to place when introducing a dependent clause? when |where| why who ?Which word is used to introduce a dependent clause that explains why something happens? when where |why| who ?Which preposition is used to add information or connect ideas? for |with| on at ?Which preposition indicates something is added to or in addition to another item? |along with| about for without ?Which conjunction links two related ideas in a sentence? but |and| or because ?Which pair of words links two related ideas in a sentence? neither...nor |both...and| either...or if...then ?What does the phrase "not only...but also" express? To give the reason To show the cause |To add information| To contrast two ideas ?Which phrase could be used to add an extra detail to something already mentioned? |what’s more| however although even though ?Which coordinating conjunction connects two related ideas and adds emphasis? or but |and| because ?Which of the following is not a coordinating conjunction used for linking related ideas? |although| and both...and neither...nor ?Which preposition means "in addition to"? with on under |along with| ?What conjunction would you use to link two related ideas, one of which adds to the other? or but |and| so ?Which adverb could be used to add more information or continue a thought? |furthermore| never hardly seldom ?Which phrase would you use to express that something is also true in addition to what was just said? |too| but however never ?What would you say to emphasize a point in a conversation or writing? because |in addition| not only...but also as well as ?Which word would you use to add another reason to a point? although because |moreover| as ?Which phrase means the same as "also"? |too| therefore then always ?Which conjunction is used to show two ideas are related and equally important? or because |both...and| if ?What adverb would you use to continue an idea or add extra detail? |furthermore| however only never ?Which word introduces a dependent clause for time? why where |when| how ~Yes/No Questions Teacher: Hey, welcome back! Today we’re going to focus on Yes/No questions. These are questions that can be answered with "yes" or "no". Ready to start? Student: Yes, I’m ready! I always get confused with do and did in questions. Teacher: No worries, that’s exactly what we’ll practice! Let’s start with the present simple. To ask about habits or routines, we use the verb "do". For example: "Do you eat lunch every day?" Student: So, I use "do" when I want to talk about something I do regularly? Teacher: Exactly! If you want to ask about a habit in the present, you say: "Do you drink tea every morning?" Student: Got it! What if I want to talk about something that happened in the past? Teacher: Great question! When you ask about the past, you use "did". For example: "Did you eat lunch yesterday?" Student: So, "did" is for past actions, right? Teacher: Yes! Let’s practice. If you want to ask about someone’s current state, like how they feel, you use the verb "be". For example: "Are you tired today?" Student: So "are" is for asking about feelings or states of being right now? Teacher: That’s right! And if you want to ask about someone’s ability now, you use "can". For example: "Can you swim?" Student: Oh, so "can" is for asking about skills or abilities, in the present. Teacher: Exactly! Now, when you're asking about someone's past ability, you use "did". For example: "Did you speak English when you were younger?" Student: So, when it’s about past abilities, I use "did", not "can"? Teacher: Yes, you’re getting it! Now, let's practice the question form for present simple. To ask about someone's job, you could say: "Do you work at a hospital?" Student: And if I wanted to know about a past action, like a trip, I’d ask: "Did you go to London last year?" Teacher: Perfect! Now, let’s talk about structure. When you’re asking about someone’s present action, you use "do" for I/we/you/they, and "does" for he/she/it. For example: "Does he play tennis?" Student: So, if I want to ask about I/we/you/they, I use "do"; for he/she/it, I use "does". Is that right? Teacher: Yes! For "he", you’d say: "Does he play tennis?" Student: And if I wanted to ask about the past, I’d always use "did". For example: "Did he play tennis yesterday?" Teacher: Exactly! Now, what if you want to ask about someone’s preferences or likes? For present preferences, you would say: "Do you like pizza?" Student: So "Do you like" is how I ask about someone’s preferences in the present, right? Teacher: Yes! Now, let’s talk about a common mistake. We don’t say "Does she likes pizza?". The correct way i Student: "Does she like pizza?" "Like" stays in the base form. Student: Ah, I see! You don’t add "s" with "does". What if I wanted to ask about something in the past? Teacher: Good question! For the past, you would say: "Did she like pizza when she was a child?" Notice the verb stays in the base form after "did". Student: Okay, so no "s" after "did" either. Teacher: Exactly! Now, let’s look at another common mistake. You shouldn’t say: "Is they watching TV?" The correct question i Student: "Are they watching TV?" Student: So, I only use "is" with singular subjects like he, she, and it, right? Teacher: Yes, and "are" is for plural subjects like they and you. Student: What if I want to ask about someone’s current job? Would I say: "Are you working at a hospital?"? Teacher: Yes! That’s a perfect example. And if you want to ask about someone’s past experience, like being in a place, you could say: "Were you in London last summer?" Student: Oh, so for past states or experiences, I use "were". Is that right? Teacher: Yes, exactly! Now let’s quickly go over the structures we’ve learned: For the present, use "do" or "does". For the past, use "did". For abilities, use "can" for present and "did" for past. For states of being, use the verb "be": "Are you happy?" or "Were you happy yesterday?" Student: I think I understand now! So, I need to remember the auxiliary verbs—do, does, did, and be—and the correct structure for asking questions. Teacher: Exactly! Keep practicing, and soon it will feel natural. Great job today!~ Concept Quiz ?Which question form would you use to ask about a habit or routine in the present? Does he play basketball? ~Wh-Questions Teacher: Hey, welcome back! Today we’re going to focus on Wh-Questions. These are questions that start with words like who, what, where, why, how, etc. They help you get more specific information. Ready? Student: Yes, I’m ready! I’ve always been confused about which Wh-word to use for different situations. Teacher: Don’t worry, we’ll go through it step-by-step! First, let’s talk about who. We use "who" to ask about people. For example: "Who called you yesterday?" Student: Ah, so "who" is for asking about a person, right? Teacher: Exactly! Now, let’s move on to "what". We use "what" to ask about things or objects. For example: "What is your favorite food?" Student: So, I use "what" when I want to know about an object, not a person? Teacher: Correct! Now, to ask about a specific person in the past, we use "who" in a past tense question. For example: "Who called you yesterday?" or "Who did you meet?" Student: Okay, so "who" stays the same even if it’s in the past? Teacher: Yes, "who" never changes. But remember, when we ask about a past action, we use "did". For example: "What did you do last weekend?" Student: Got it! What if I want to ask about a place? Teacher: Good question! When you want to ask about a place, we use "where". For example: "Where do you live?" Student: Oh, so "where" is for places, like asking where someone lives. Teacher: Yes! Now, let’s talk about "when". If you want to ask about time, you use "when". For example: "When is the meeting?" Student: So, I use "when" for time-related questions. What if I want to ask about why something is happening? Teacher: To ask why something is happening, we use "why". For example: "Why are you late?" Student: That makes sense! How about asking how something happens or works? Teacher: For that, you use "how". For example: "How does this work?" or "How do you make pizza?" Student: Ah, so "how" is for asking about methods or processes. Teacher: Exactly! Now, let’s look at "which". We use "which" when you are choosing from a specific set of options. For example: "Which color do you like?" Student: So, "which" is used when there are specific options to choose from? Teacher: Yes, exactly! Now, what if you want to know about the frequency of something, like how often it happens? You would ask: "How often do you exercise?" Student: So "how often" is for asking about frequency, like how many times something happens? Teacher: Exactly! Now, to ask about a quantity of countable things, like books or apples, we use "how many". For example: "How many books do you have?" Student: So, "how many" is for countable things, like numbers of objects? Teacher: Yes! But for uncountable things, like water or money, we use "how much". For example: "How much money do you have?" Student: Got it! "How much" for uncountable things and "how many" for countable things. Teacher: Exactly! Now, let’s practice with "how long" for asking about duration. For example: "How long does it take to get there?" Student: So "how long" asks about how much time something takes? Teacher: Exactly! Now, to ask about someone’s height, you would say: "How tall are you?" Student: Oh, so "how tall" is for asking about height. What about speed? Teacher: For speed, you ask: "How fast can you run?" Student: So, "how fast" is for asking about speed or how quickly something happens? Teacher: Yes! Now, let’s talk about "why" again. If you want to ask about a reason, like why someone is laughing, you ask: "Why are you laughing?" Student: Ah, so "why" asks about reasons or motives. What about asking how someone feels? Teacher: For that, we use: "How are you?" or "How are you today?" Student: Oh, so "how" can also ask about feelings or conditions. Teacher: Exactly! Now, to ask about someone’s profession, you would ask: "What is your job?" or "Where do you work?" Student: So, if I want to ask about someone's job, I can use "what" or "where", depending on the context? Teacher: Yes, exactly! Lastly, if you want to ask about a person’s residence, you’d ask: "Where do you live?" Student: Perfect! So, I just need to remember the right Wh-word for each situation. Teacher: Exactly! Here’s a quick recap: Who – for a person. What – for things or objects. Where – for places. When – for time. Why – for reasons. How – for methods, conditions, or speed. Which – for choosing from options. How many – for countable things. How much – for uncountable things. How often – for frequency. How long – for duration. Student: Got it! This really helped. Thanks! Teacher: You're welcome! Keep practicing and it’ll be easy soon!~ Concept Quiz ?Which question is used to ask about a person? |Who called you?| What is your favorite color? Where do you live? Why do you study English? ~Conditionals Teacher: Welcome back! Today, we’re going to talk about conditionals. These are sentences that express possibilities or hypothetical situations, depending on the structure. There are four main types of conditional Student: Zero Conditional, First Conditional, Second Conditional, and Third Conditional. Ready to dive in? Student: Yes! But, I’m not sure when to use each one. Can you explain? Teacher: Absolutely. Let’s start with the Zero Conditional. We use this for general truths or facts. These are things that are always true. For example: "If you heat water to 100°C, it boils." Student: So, the Zero Conditional is for facts that always happen under certain conditions? Teacher: Exactly! And in the Zero Conditional, we use the present simple in both the if-clause and the main clause. Here's a negative form: "If you don’t heat water to 100°C, it doesn’t boil." Student: Oh, I see. So, it’s simple – if something happens, something else always happens. Teacher: Yes! Now, let’s move to the First Conditional. This is used for real, future situations that are likely to happen. For example: "If it rains tomorrow, we will cancel the trip." Student: So, we use the First Conditional to talk about real possibilities in the future? Teacher: Yes, that's right! In the First Conditional, the if-clause uses the present simple, and the main clause uses will. Now, let’s look at the negative form: "If it doesn’t rain tomorrow, we won’t cancel the trip." Student: Okay, so it's about the future, and it's a real situation that could happen. Teacher: Exactly. Now, the Second Conditional is used for hypothetical or imaginary situations in the present or future—things that aren’t true now but we imagine them. For example: "If I had a car, I would drive to work." Student: So, the Second Conditional talks about things that aren’t true now, but could happen in an imagined situation? Teacher: Exactly! In the Second Conditional, the if-clause uses the past simple, and the main clause uses would. If you want to negate it, you could say: "If I didn’t have a car, I wouldn’t drive to work." Student: So, this one is for imagined situations or things we wish were different in the present. Teacher: Exactly. Finally, let’s talk about the Third Conditional. This one is for talking about past situations that didn’t happen, and the missed opportunities or regrets that come with them. For example: "If I had studied, I would have passed the test." Student: So, the Third Conditional talks about what could have happened in the past, but didn’t? Teacher: Yes! In the Third Conditional, the if-clause uses the past perfect, and the main clause uses would have + past participle. For example, "If I had studied harder, I would have passed the exam." Student: Ah, so this is for past regrets and things that didn’t happen. Teacher: Exactly. Now, let's practice! Which conditional would you use to express a fact that’s always true? Student: That would be the Zero Conditional, right? Teacher: Correct! And what about talking about a real possibility in the future? Student: That would be the First Conditional. Teacher: Yes! And how about if I want to talk about a hypothetical situation, like something unreal now? Student: That’s the Second Conditional. Teacher: Exactly. And if you’re talking about a past situation that didn’t happen and you regret it? Student: That’s the Third Conditional! Teacher: Great job! Let’s go over some common expressions used in each conditional: Zero Conditional: We often use "when" or "whenever". For example: "When you heat water to 100°C, it boils." First Conditional: We use "tomorrow," "next week," or "if it rains." For example: "If it rains tomorrow, we will cancel the picnic." Second Conditional: You often hear "if I were you" or "in your place." For example: "If I were you, I would take that job." Third Conditional: We use it to talk about missed opportunities in the past, like "I should have" or "If only." For example: "If only I had studied harder, I would have passed." Student: That’s super helpful! So, the key is to remember the time frame (present, future, past) and whether it’s real or hypothetical. Teacher: Exactly! Keep practicing, and you’ll get the hang of it. Let’s finish with a little quiz—can you tell me which conditional this sentence is? Student: Okay! Let’s go! Teacher: "If I had known, I would have studied." Student: That’s the Third Conditional! Teacher: Excellent! You’re all set to take the quiz. These are ways to talk about things we wish were different in the present, past, or future. Student: That sounds interesting! But, I'm not really sure how to use them correctly. Teacher: No worries! Let's break it down. We’ll start with wishes about the present. When you want to express a wish about something that isn't true right now, you can say: "I wish + subject + verb (past form)." Student: So, if I want to say that I wish I were on vacation, I’d say: "I wish I was on vacation."? Teacher: Exactly! But remember, when you’re talking about a present unreal situation, it’s actually more common to use "were" instead of "was," even with "I" or "he/she." So, you'd say: "I wish I were on vacation." Student: Got it! So, "I wish" is used for things that aren’t real now? Teacher: That’s right! Now, let's talk about regrets about the past. To express a regret, we use "I wish + subject + had + past participle." For example: "I wish I had studied harder for the exam." Student: Oh, so "had + past participle" is for things I didn’t do in the past, but I wish I had? Teacher: Exactly! It's a way to show you regret that something didn’t happen. Here's another example: "If only I had gone to bed earlier." Student: So, "If only" works the same way as "I wish" for regrets? Teacher: Yes, "If only" is another way to express regret about the past, but it’s a little more emotional or stronger. You can say: "If only I had studied more." or "If only I had known about the party." Student: Okay, so "If only" is a stronger way to express regret about past actions? Teacher: Yes! Now, let’s move to expressing wishes about the future. If you want to talk about a future unreal situation, we use: "I wish + subject + would + verb (base form)." Student: So, if I want to say that I hope the weather changes, I could say: "I wish it would stop raining." Teacher: Perfect! This is used for things you hope will happen in the future, but it’s not certain. So, if you’re wishing for a change, you use "would." Student: So, the structure for wishes about the present is "I wish + past form," for wishes about the future, it’s "I wish + would + verb," and for regrets about the past, it’s "I wish + had + past participle"? Teacher: Exactly! You’ve got it. Now, let’s practice with a few sentences. How would you express a wish about the present for something you can’t do right now? Student: "I wish I could play the piano." Teacher: That’s correct! You’re wishing for a present ability that you don’t have. Now, how about a regret about the past? Student: "I wish I had gone to the beach last weekend." Teacher: Perfect! Now, how would you express a future wish that’s unlikely to happen? Student: "I wish I could travel to Paris." Teacher: Exactly! That’s an unlikely hope about the future. And what about a strong regret about the past using "If only"? Student: "If only I had studied harder for the test." Teacher: Excellent! Now, one last thing. Sometimes we want to make a complaint about the present. We can say: "I wish + subject + verb (past form)" to show frustration or dissatisfaction. For example: "I wish I could find my keys." Student: Ah, so you’re saying "I wish" is not just for regrets or hopes, but also for complaints? Teacher: Exactly! It’s a very flexible expression. You can use it to talk about unreal situations, things you regret, and things that annoy you in the present. Student: This is really clear now! So, when we talk about things that aren’t true now, we use "I wish + past verb", for regrets about the past, we use "I wish + had + past participle", and for future hopes that are unlikely, we use "I wish + would". Teacher: Perfect! Now, let's do a quick review. What’s the structure for a wish about the present? Student: "I wish + past verb." Teacher: Great! And how do we express a regret about the past? Student: "I wish + had + past participle." Teacher: And for a future wish that’s unlikely? Student: "I wish + would + verb." Teacher: You’ve got it! You’re ready for the quiz now. It's a complaint about a present situation. |It's a wish for something that did not happen in the past.| It's an expression of happiness about the present. ~Unless Teacher: Alright, today we’re going to talk about the word "unless" and how to use it. "Unless" is used to express a condition that must be true for something else to happen. It means "if not" or "except if." Student: So, it's like "if," but the opposite, right? Teacher: Exactly! Let's start with the basic meaning. "Unless" means "if not." For example, if you say, "Unless it rains tomorrow, we will go hiking," you’re saying, "If it does not rain tomorrow, we will go hiking." Student: Ah, so it’s like the negative condition. Teacher: Yes, exactly. Now, let’s look at how we structure sentences with "unless." The pattern i Student: "Unless + subject + verb, [result]." For example, "Unless you study, you will fail the test." Student: So, "unless you study" means "if you don't study"? Teacher: Yes, and the result i Student: "you will fail the test." Now, let’s practice with a negative sentence. You can say: "Unless I leave, I will be late." This means, "If I don't leave, I will be late." Student: I see! So, we use the present simple with "unless" when we’re talking about real or possible situations, like things that can really happen. Teacher: Correct! Let’s talk about time expressions. You can use "unless" with time expressions like "tomorrow," "next week," and "now." For example: "Unless we finish the project by tomorrow, we won’t meet the deadline." Student: So, it’s wrong to say: "Unless we will finish the project tomorrow," right? Teacher: Exactly! You don’t need "will" after "unless." It’s not needed in the condition part. Now, how would you form a question using "unless"? Student: Would it be: "What will happen unless you study?" Teacher: Yes, great! You’re asking about the condition and its result. Now, let’s take a look at how we use "unless" in a 1st conditional sentence. For example, "Unless you arrive late, we will start the meeting." It’s used to talk about real situations in the future. Student: So, "unless" is used to express something that might happen, and if that doesn't happen, something else will. Like, if I don’t arrive late, we’ll start the meeting. Teacher: Exactly! Now, let’s talk about mistakes. Which of the following is incorrect with "unless"? Unless you work harder, you won’t pass the course. Unless she study more, she will not pass the test. Unless they help, we won’t finish on time. Unless you do it, we won’t complete the task. Student: I think b) is wrong. It should be "Unless she studies more." Teacher: Right! We need to use the third person singular form "studies," not "study." Now, let’s check out a negative structure: "Unless you don’t study, you will fail the exam." This is incorrect, because after "unless," we don’t use "don't." The correct version i Student: "Unless you study, you will fail the exam." Student: So, "unless" means "if not," but we can’t have a double negative like "unless you don't." Teacher: Exactly! Now, let’s look at another time expression example: "Unless we finish the project by tomorrow, we won’t meet the deadline." You’re saying that if we don’t finish the project before tomorrow, the deadline won’t be met. Student: So, we use "unless" for both conditions and time limits! Teacher: Exactly. You’re doing great! Now, let’s practice forming the affirmative with "unless." Here’s a sentence: "Unless we leave early, we won’t make it to the airport on time." Can you create one like this? Student: "Unless I leave soon, I will miss my flight." Teacher: Perfect! You’ve got it. And if you want to express a negative result with "unless," you can say: "Unless he eats, he will be hungry." Student: Ah, so it’ Student: "unless + subject + verb (present form), result." Teacher: Exactly! And now, a little tricky question: What does "unless" mean? If |If not| Because As long as Student: It's b) If not! Teacher: Exactly! Well done! You’re now ready for your quiz on "unless." Great job today!~ Concept Quiz ?What does "unless" mean? Unless she calls, I will go to the party. ~Talking About Time Teacher: Today, we’re going to focus on using time expressions, prepositions, and adverbs to describe when things happen. Understanding these will help you talk more clearly about the present, past, and future. Ready? Student: Yes, I’m ready! Let’s start! Teacher: Great! Let’s begin with adverbs. Adverbs help us describe when something happens. For example, "already" refers to something that has happened before now. Student: So if I say, "I’ve already finished my homework," it means I did it earlier? Teacher: Exactly! Now, what about "yet"? It means something has not happened up to now, but it might still happen. For example: "I haven’t finished my homework yet." Student: Oh, so "yet" is used with negative sentences? Teacher: Yes, that’s right! Now, let’s move to future time. When we want to talk about something happening soon after now, we use "soon." For example, "I will call you soon." Student: So "soon" means in a little while, right? Teacher: Exactly! It shows something will happen in the near future. Now, how about "gradually"? It means something happens step-by-step or over a long period. For example: "The weather is getting better gradually." Student: It’s like things change slowly, bit by bit. Teacher: Yes! And now, let’s talk about "finally." We use this when something happens after a long wait. Like: "Finally, I finished my project!" Student: So it’s like the end of something, after a long time? Teacher: Yes, exactly! Now, what about using "now"? It refers to the present moment. For example, "I’m studying now." Student: So, "now" is for what’s happening right now. Teacher: Correct! And when something is not yet finished or still ongoing, we use "still." For example: "I am still working on my homework." Student: So if I say, "I’m still waiting for the bus," it means I haven’t finished waiting yet? Teacher: Yes, that’s perfect! Now, let’s talk about prepositions. For example, when we talk about a specific day of the week, we use "on." For instance: "I will meet you on Monday." Student: So, not "in Monday" or "at Monday," right? Teacher: Exactly! "On" is used for days. What about talking about a month or a year? For that, we use "in." For example: "I was born in July." Student: So we say "in July" but "on Monday." Got it! Teacher: Great! Now, when we talk about a duration of time, like how long something lasts, we use "for." For example: "I worked for two hours." Student: So, "for" tells us how long something lasts? Teacher: Yes, exactly! And when we talk about starting something in the past and continuing until now, we use "since." For example: "I’ve lived here since 2010." Student: So, "since" is used for the starting point of a time period, like a year or a specific moment? Teacher: That’s right! Now, let’s talk about deadlines. To describe a time limit, we use "by." For example: "You need to finish your homework by 5 PM." Student: So, "by" means the latest time something can happen? Teacher: Exactly! And what if we want to talk about an event that happens within a specific time frame? We use "within." For example: "I’ll finish the report within two hours." Student: Ah, so "within" tells us that something will happen during a certain period, not after. Teacher: Exactly! Now, let’s move on to conjunctions. For actions that happen at the same time, we use "while." For example: "I was listening to music while I was studying." Student: So, "while" means two things happen together? Teacher: Yes, exactly. And when we talk about an event happening after another event, we use "after." For example: "I will go shopping after I finish my homework." Student: So, the second event happens later than the first event. Teacher: Yes! And another common conjunction is "as soon as." This means immediately after something happens. For example: "I’ll call you as soon as I finish." Student: So, it’s like saying, "right when I finish, I will call you." Teacher: Exactly! Now, let’s talk about prepositions for time periods. If something happens in the middle of a time period, we use "during." For example: "I met her during my vacation." Student: So, "during" is used when something happens inside a time period? Teacher: Exactly! And to talk about waiting for someone or something until a certain time, we use "until." For example: "I will wait until she arrives." Student: So, I’m waiting for the event to happen before I do something else. Teacher: Yes! Now, let’s finish with a time expression: "the day after." This means the day following today. For example: "I will meet you the day after tomorrow." Student: So, that’s not today, and it’s not tomorrow—it’s the day after tomorrow! Teacher: Exactly! Now, let’s review a bit. What’s the right preposition for a specific day of the week? Student: We use "on". For example, "I will visit you on Monday." Teacher: Great! And what about a future event? How do we say, "I plan to meet my friends?" Student: You can say, "I am going to meet my friends this evening." Teacher: Perfect! And finally, when talking about past habits or things you used to do, we use "used to." For example: "I used to go to the park every weekend." Student: So, "used to" is for things that happened regularly in the past, but don’t happen anymore? Teacher: Exactly! Well done! You’re ready for the quiz now.~ Concept Quiz ?Which word refers to an action that has already happened before now? yet |already| now still ?Which adverb means an event will happen soon after now? eventually next |soon| lately ?What does "gradually" mean? I will meet my friends this evening. I met my friends this evening. ~Present Simple Teacher: Hello! Today, we’re going to focus on the Present Simple tense. This tense is used to talk about things that happen regularly or always, like habits, routines, and general truths. Let’s start by looking at how to form affirmative sentences. Student: I’m ready! Teacher: In Present Simple, we use the base form of the verb. For example, "I play tennis every weekend." The structure is Subject + verb (base form) + object. Student: So, if I say, "I play tennis," that’s correct? Teacher: Yes, that’s right! But remember, with he, she, and it, we add an -s to the verb. For example, "He plays tennis." Can you try one with she? Student: Sure! "She plays tennis." Teacher: Perfect! Now, let's look at negative sentences. To make a Present Simple sentence negative, we use "do not" or "does not". For example, "I do not play tennis" or "She does not play tennis." Student: So, with I, you, we, and they, we use "do not," and with he, she, it, we use "does not," right? Teacher: Exactly! Now, how would you make this sentence negative: "He eats pizza"? Student: It would be, "He does not eat pizza." Teacher: Perfect! Now, let’s move on to questions. To form a question in Present Simple, we start with "do" or "does" followed by the subject and then the verb (base form). For example, "Do you play tennis?" Student: So, we use "do" or "does" at the beginning? Teacher: Yes, that’s correct! And remember, for he, she, or it, we use "does". So, the question would be, "Does she play tennis?" Student: Got it! How about if I want to ask, "I play tennis"? Teacher: You would ask, "Do I play tennis?" Student: Oh! So, "do" for I, you, we, and they, and "does" for he, she, it? Teacher: Yes, exactly! Now, let’s talk about time expressions. Present Simple is often used with adverbs that show habits or repeated actions, like "always," "never," "every day," and "often." For example: "I play tennis every day." Student: So, "every day" shows a habit? Teacher: Yes, exactly! If you want to talk about an action that happens regularly, you can use expressions like "usually," "often," or "never." Student: Okay, so if I say, "She never eats vegetables," that’s a good example of a habit. Teacher: Exactly! Now, let’s check out a negative question. If you want to ask a negative question, it’s simple: Do/Does + subject + not + verb. For example, "Does he not like pizza?" Student: Oh! Or, "Don’t they go to school?" Teacher: Yes, that’s correct! Now, let’s go over verb forms again. For he, she, or it, we add -s to the verb in the affirmative form. So, "He plays," "She eats," "It rains." But for I, you, we, and they, we just use the base form. "I play," "You eat," "They rain." Student: So "plays" for he, she, it, and "play" for I, you, we, they? Teacher: Yes! Now, let’s look at present simple with "to be". For the verb to be in Present Simple, the structure changes. We use am, is, or are depending on the subject. For example: I am a teacher. She is a student. They are friends. Student: So, we use "am" for I, "is" for he, she, it, and "are" for you, we, they? Teacher: Exactly! Now, to check your understanding, let’s go over some examples. Student: Okay, let’s go! Teacher: If you want to say, "They play football every weekend," is that correct in the Present Simple? Student: Yes, because they is a plural subject, so we just use the base form "play." Teacher: Correct! Now, how about this sentence: "She do not like chocolate." Student: That’s wrong. It should be, "She does not like chocolate." Teacher: Right! Now, let’s form a question: "He eats breakfast at 7 AM." Student: The question is, "Does he eat breakfast at 7 AM?" Teacher: Yes, exactly! And how would you say, "I work every day" in the negative? Student: "I do not work every day." Teacher: Great! So, Present Simple is for regular actions, routines, and general facts. It’s not used for actions happening right now, but for things that happen regularly or are always true. Student: Got it! So, I use Present Simple to talk about my habits or things that are always true, like, "The Earth revolves around the Sun." Teacher: Exactly! You’ve got it. I think you’re ready for the quiz!~ Concept Quiz ?Which of the following sentences follows the correct structure for the affirmative form of Present Simple? |Subject + verb (base form) + object.| Subject + do/does + verb (base form) + object. Now Never |Every day| Tomorrow ~Past Simple Teacher: Hi there! Today, we’re going to work on the Past Simple tense. This tense is used to talk about actions that happened at a specific time in the past, like yesterday, last year, or in 2010. Student: Sounds good! I’m ready. Teacher: Great! Let's start with the affirmative form. The structure for Past Simple i Student: Subject + verb (past form) + object. For example, "I played football yesterday." Student: So, we just add -ed to the verb? Teacher: That’s right! Most verbs are regular, so you just add -ed to the base form. For example: "He worked late last night." But, some verbs are irregular, like "go". The past form is "went", not "goed". Student: Okay, so "I went to the park" is correct? Teacher: Yes, exactly! Now, for negative sentences, we use "did not" or "didn't" before the verb in its base form. For example, "I did not play football yesterday." Student: So, we don’t add -ed to the verb in the negative? Teacher: Correct! The verb stays in the base form after "did". For example, "She didn’t eat lunch" or "We didn't go to the party." Student: Got it. And for questions? Teacher: For questions, we use "Did" at the beginning. The structure i Student: Did + subject + verb (base form)? For example: "Did you play football yesterday?" Student: So, the question is "Did" plus the base form, not the past form of the verb? Teacher: Exactly! Here’s another example: "Did they go to the park?" Student: Okay! What about the sentence "He did play football yesterday"? Is that correct? Teacher: Hmm, that’s not quite right. We don’t need "did" with the verb in the affirmative form. You should just say: "He played football yesterday." Student: So, "did" only goes in negative sentences and questions? Teacher: Yes, that’s right! Let’s move on to time expressions. The Past Simple is often used with specific time expressions like "yesterday," "last week," "in 2010," and "an hour ago." Student: So, I can say, "I saw her yesterday"? Teacher: Exactly! Or, "We visited Paris last year." But remember, we don’t use the Past Simple with time expressions like "soon" or "tomorrow." Student: Oh, because those are future events? Teacher: Yes, exactly! Now, let’s look at how to form a negative question. The structure i Student: Did + subject + not + verb (base form)? For example: "Did they not go to the party?" Student: So it’s similar to a regular question, but we add "not" after "did"? Teacher: Exactly! Another way to ask a negative question i Student: "Didn’t they go to the party?" It’s just a shorter form. Student: Ah, I see. And how would you correct this sentence? "He didn’t played tennis yesterday." Teacher: You should say: "He didn’t play tennis yesterday." We never add -ed to the verb after "did". Student: Oh, okay. No -ed after "didn’t". That’s important to remember! Teacher: Yes, that’s a common mistake! Now, let’s practice with some irregular verbs. The verb "eat" becomes "ate" in the past, and "go" becomes "went." Can you try making sentences with those? Student: Sure! "I ate breakfast this morning," and "I went to the cinema last weekend." Teacher: Excellent! Now, let’s look at a wrong sentence: "I don’t saw her yesterday." Student: Oh! That’s wrong because "don’t" is for Present Simple. It should be, "I didn’t see her yesterday." Teacher: Exactly! Great job. So, remember, in Past Simple, use "did" for questions and negatives, and add -ed for regular verbs in affirmative sentences. Irregular verbs are a bit trickier, but once you practice, they’ll get easier! Student: I think I understand! One last question: What’s the correct form for the verb "read" in the past? Is it "readed" or "read"? Teacher: Good question! The past form of "read" is the same as the presen Teacher: "read". But it’s pronounced differently. In the present, we say /riːd/, but in the past, it’s pronounced /rɛd/. Student: Ah, so it’s pronounced differently! Okay, I think I’m ready for the quiz now. Teacher: You’re doing great! They visited the museum last week. We played football every Sunday. |He will study for the exam tomorrow.| She watched TV yesterday. ~Present Continuous Teacher: Hi! Today we’re going to go over the Present Continuous tense. This tense is used to describe actions that are happening right now, around the present time, or even to talk about future plans. Student: Okay, sounds interesting! What’s the structure for Present Continuous? Teacher: Good question! The affirmative structure is: Subject + am/is/are + verb (ing form) + object. For example: "I am eating dinner right now." or "They are playing football." Student: So, we use am, is, or are depending on the subject? Teacher: Exactly! We use am for "I," is for "he, she, it," and are for "you, we, they." Student: Got it! How do we form a negative sentence in Present Continuous? Teacher: To make a negative, just add not after the auxiliary verb (am, is, are). So the structure is: Subject + am/is/are + not + verb (ing form) + object. For example: "She is not playing the piano" or "We are not going to the party." Student: So, it’s "is not" or "isn't" and "are not" or "aren’t", right? Teacher: Yes, exactly! You can use the short form like "isn’t" or "aren’t" to sound more natural. Student: Cool! What about questions? How do we ask a question with Present Continuous? Teacher: For questions, we invert the auxiliary verb (am/is/are) with the subject. The structure is: Am/Is/Are + subject + verb (ing form) + object? For example: "Is she playing the piano?" or "Are they coming to the party?" Student: So, for a question, we put am/is/are first? Teacher: Yes, exactly! And remember, the verb is always in the -ing form. You can’t use the base form of the verb like in Present Simple. Student: That makes sense. So, what's the difference between Present Continuous and Present Simple? Teacher: Great question! Present Continuous describes actions happening right now or temporary actions. Present Simple, on the other hand, is for habits, routines, or general facts. Student: Oh, so I’d use Present Continuous for things like "I am studying English now" and Present Simple for things like "I study English every day." Teacher: Exactly! Now, let’s talk about time expressions. For Present Continuous, we often use expressions like: right now, at the moment, currently, or today. For example, "I am reading a book right now." Student: What about tomorrow? Can we use Present Continuous for that? Teacher: Yes, absolutely! You can also use Present Continuous for future plans that are already decided or arranged. For example: "I am meeting my friends tomorrow." or "We are going to the cinema tonight." Student: So if I say, "I am going to the park tomorrow," that’s correct? Teacher: Exactly! It’s a future plan. But remember, we don’t use Present Continuous for future predictions or things we can’t control, like "It is raining tomorrow." That's just for planned actions. Student: Got it! I think I understand the basics now. But what about stative verbs? Can we use those with Present Continuous? Teacher: Ah, good point! Some verbs are stative and describe states or feelings, and we generally don't use these in Present Continuous. For example, "I know the answer" or "She likes chocolate." These actions aren't usually happening right now. But for dynamic verbs like play, study, work, you can use them in Present Continuous because they describe actions. Student: So, saying "I am knowing the answer" would be incorrect? Teacher: Exactly! It should just be "I know the answer." Stative verbs stay in the Present Simple. Student: Okay! Now, if I say, "They are play football right now," is that correct? Teacher: Almost! The correct form i Student: "They are playing football right now." You need to add -ing to the verb. Student: Got it! And how about the question, "She is play the piano?" Teacher: That’s not quite right either. The correct question i Student: "Is she playing the piano?" Remember, you need the auxiliary verb "is" before the subject and the verb must be in the -ing form. Student: I see now. And how do we form a negative question in Present Continuous? Teacher: For negative questions, you just add not after am/is/are. So, for example: "Aren’t they playing football?" or "Isn’t she working today?" Student: Oh, that's easy! So, no -ing mistake, just remember to use am/is/are + not? Teacher: Exactly! You’ve got it. Now, let's check a few more sentences: Teacher: Which one is incorrect in Present Continuous? She is studying for the test right now. We are meeting our friends later today. They are play football at the moment. I am cooking dinner right now. Student: The third one, "They are play football at the moment." should be "are playing," right? Teacher: Yes! That’s the right correction. Good job! Student: This is making sense now. But what about using the Present Continuous for something that happens always? Is that okay? Teacher: Good question! We don't use Present Continuous for habits or always actions. For example, you wouldn’t say, "I am always drinking coffee." You should use the Present Simple there: "I always drink coffee." Student: Got it! So, Present Continuous is for things happening now or temporary actions, not things that happen regularly. Teacher: Exactly! Great job today! Do you reading the book? You reading the book? ~Past Continuous Teacher: Hi there! Today, we’re going to dive into the Past Continuous tense. This tense is used to describe actions that were happening at a specific moment in the past or actions that were interrupted by another event. Student: Oh, that sounds interesting! How do we form Past Continuous? Teacher: The affirmative structure is: Subject + was/were + verb (ing form) + object. For example: "I was playing football when it started to rain." Student: So, we use was for singular subjects like I, he, she, and it, and were for we, you, and they, right? Teacher: Exactly! Now, let’s look at the negative form. To make it negative, we add not after was or were. The structure is: Subject + was/were + not + verb (ing form) + object. For example: "I was not watching TV at 10 PM." Student: So, for the negative, it’s was not or wasn’t and were not or weren’t, right? Teacher: Yes, that’s correct! You can use the short form like wasn’t or weren’t to sound more natural. Student: Okay, and how do we make a question in Past Continuous? Teacher: To form a question, we invert was/were with the subject. So, the structure is: Was/Were + subject + verb (ing form) + object? For example: "Was she working yesterday at 5 PM?" Student: Ah, so the auxiliary verb was/were comes at the beginning? Teacher: Yes, that’s right! Always remember, the main verb is in the -ing form. Student: I see. And when do we use Past Continuous? Teacher: Past Continuous is used to describe actions that were happening at a specific time in the past or when one action was interrupted by another. For example: I was studying when the phone rang. Here, the action of studying was interrupted by the phone ringing. Student: So, Past Continuous is for actions in progress in the past? Teacher: Yes! It’s for ongoing actions. It’s like a snapshot of what was happening at that moment. Student: Got it! Can we use Past Continuous for repeated actions in the past? Teacher: No, Past Continuous isn’t for repeated actions. For repeated actions in the past, we use Past Simple. For example, "I played football every Saturday" is Past Simple. Student: So, for Past Continuous, we’d say "I was playing football when it started to rain." Is that right? Teacher: Exactly! That’s the correct use. Now, if two actions were happening at the same time, we can use Past Continuous for both actions. For example: I was reading when she called me. Both actions were happening together. Student: Oh, I see. And what about describing a recently completed action? Teacher: Great question! For recently completed actions, we don’t use Past Continuous. We use Past Simple. For example: I studied and now I’m tired. No Teacher: "I was studying and now I’m tired." Student: So, we wouldn’t say, "I was studied," right? Teacher: Correct! The verb needs to be in the -ing form for Past Continuous, but "studied" is in the Past Simple form. Student: Got it! And what about time expressions? What kind of time expressions do we use with Past Continuous? Teacher: We use time expressions like at that moment, while, when, and at 5 PM yesterday. For example: I was working at 8 PM yesterday. She was cooking when I arrived. Student: So, we use while and when to talk about two actions happening together, right? Teacher: Exactly! For example, "I was reading while she was cooking." Two actions happening at the same time in the past. Student: What about a sentence like, "He was play football when I called him"? Teacher: That’s incorrect. It should be: He was playing football when I called him. Remember, we always use the -ing form in Past Continuous. Student: Oh, right! So, we don’t use the base form of the verb. Teacher: Exactly. It’s was/were + verb (ing). Now, let’s try another example: Which one is correct? They were studying when the bell rang. They was studying when the bell rang. Which one is wrong? Student: Option b is wrong because it should be "were", not "was". Teacher: Correct! Nice job. And here’s another one: I was sleeping when you called me last night. I sleep when you called me last night. Which one is correct? Student: It’s the first one: "I was sleeping when you called me last night." Teacher: Right again! Past Continuous is for actions in progress in the past, and Past Simple is for the action that interrupted that progress. Student: So, if I say, "I was eating when the phone rang," I’m showing that the eating was happening, and then the phone rang, right? Teacher: Exactly! And let’s check your understanding with a final question: Which one is incorrect? I was eating when he called. He didn’t was sleeping at 7 PM. She was running when it started to rain. They were playing basketball when I arrived. Student: Option b is incorrect. It should be "didn’t was sleeping" is wrong. It should be: "He wasn’t sleeping at 7 PM." Teacher: Perfect! You’ve got it. Right now |While| Never Always ?What is the correct form of Past Continuous in this sentence: They __________ (work) at 8 PM yesterday. worked |was working| |were working| working ~Present Perfect Teacher: Hello! Today, we’re going to explore the Present Perfect tense. It's very useful because it helps us talk about actions that have a connection to the present but were completed at unspecified times in the past. Ready? Student: Yes, sounds interesting! How do we form Present Perfect? Teacher: Good question! The structure for the affirmative form is: Subject + has/have + past participle (V3). For example: "I have visited Paris." Student: Oh, so we use have for I, you, we, they and has for he, she, it? Teacher: Exactly! You got it. Now, let’s talk about the negative form. For the negative, we add not after have or has. The structure is: Subject + has/have + not + past participle (V3). For example: "I haven’t finished my homework." Student: So it’s haven’t for I, you, we, they, and hasn’t for he, she, it? Teacher: Yes, that’s right! Now, what about making a question? Student: Hmm, I’m guessing it starts with have or has, right? Teacher: Exactly! The structure for questions is: Have/Has + subject + past participle (V3) + object? For example: "Have you seen that movie?" Student: Got it! So, the question form is the same as the affirmative, but with the words have or has at the beginning. Teacher: Perfect! Now, let’s talk about some time expressions we use with Present Perfect. One important thing is duration — to talk about how long something has been happening. We often use for and since. Student: Can you explain that? Teacher: Sure! For is used with a duration of time: "I have lived here for five years." Since is used with a starting point: "I have lived here since 2010." Teacher: "I have lived here since 2010." Student: So, if I want to say that something started in the past and continues to the present, I can use for or since? Teacher: Yes, exactly! And we can also use words like just, yet, already, and ever to add more detail. Student: Can you give me examples with those? Teacher: Of course! Just is used to show a recently completed action. For example: "I have just finished my lunch." Yet is used in negative sentences and questions to talk about things that haven’t happened yet. For example: Negative: "I haven’t finished my homework yet." Question: "Have you finished the report yet?" Already shows that something has happened sooner than expected. For example: "I have already seen that movie." Ever is used in questions to talk about experiences at any time up to now. For example: "Have you ever been to Paris?" Student: So, with Present Perfect, we don’t care about exact times, just the connection to the present? Teacher: Exactly! It's all about actions that have an impact on the present, not when they happened. We don’t need to specify a time. For example, "I have visited Paris" means at some point in the past, I visited Paris, and it’s relevant to now. Student: Oh, so I shouldn’t say "I visited Paris last year," because that’s a specific time, right? Teacher: Yes! You would use Past Simple for a specific time in the past, like "I visited Paris last year." But for Present Perfect, the time is unspecified. Student: Got it. And what about "never"? Teacher: Great question! We use never with Present Perfect to show that something has not happened at any point in the past. For example: "I have never been to Japan." Student: So, if I wanted to say I haven't gone to the store, I would say, "I have never gone to the store"? Teacher: Exactly! Student: How about if I wanted to use "yet" in a negative sentence? Teacher: You would say: "I haven’t gone to the store yet." Yet shows that you’re expecting it to happen, but it hasn't happened at the present moment. Student: And for "ever" in a question? Teacher: You would ask: "Have you ever been to Paris?" This asks about your experience up to now, without a specific time. Student: I see! So, Present Perfect is all about talking about things that started in the past and are still relevant to now. Teacher: Exactly! And it’s used for uncompleted actions, general experiences, or actions that happened at an unspecified time. We use time expressions like for, since, yet, already, just, and ever to give more context. Student: And for something that just finished, I would say, "I have just finished my work." Right? Teacher: Correct! That’s a perfect example. Student: What about something that’s been happening for a while? Teacher: For an ongoing action or unfinished state, you would use: I have lived here for five years. This shows that the action started in the past and is still happening now. Student: Got it! Now I feel ready for the quiz. I am reading that book. I will read that book. ~Past Perfect Teacher: Hi! Today, we're going to dive into the Past Perfect tense. This one is really helpful when we want to show that one action happened before another action in the past. Ready to learn? Student: Yes, I’m excited! What’s the main purpose of Past Perfect? Teacher: The main purpose of the Past Perfect is to describe an action that was completed before another action in the past. For example: "I had finished my homework when my friend called." The action of finishing the homework happened before the friend called. Student: So, it’s about two actions in the past, and one happened before the other, right? Teacher: Exactly! Now let’s look at the structure. For the affirmative sentence, we use: Subject + had + past participle (V3). For example: "She had finished her homework before dinner." Student: And for the negative sentence? Teacher: For the negative, we add not after had: Subject + had + not + past participle (V3). For example: "I had not finished my homework when she arrived." Student: So, it’s had not for the negative form. How do we ask questions in Past Perfect? Teacher: Great question! For a question, the structure is: Had + subject + past participle (V3) + object? For example: "Had you finished your work before the meeting?" Student: Oh, so "had" comes at the beginning of the question? Teacher: Exactly. Now let’s talk about time expressions we often use with Past Perfect. We use words like before, already, and by the time to show the relationship between two actions. For example: Before: "I had left before they arrived." Already: "She had already eaten when I got home." By the time: "By the time I arrived, they had left." Student: So, Past Perfect is all about showing that one action was completed before another action in the past. But can you give me an example where something wasn’t finished yet? Teacher: Of course! If an action wasn't finished by the time something else happened in the past, we can use Past Perfect like this: I had not finished my homework when my friend arrived. This means the homework wasn’t finished before the friend arrived. Student: Ah, so it's like saying, "I didn’t finish it before something else happened"? Teacher: Yes, exactly! Now, let's look at an example where an action was interrupted. For example: "I had been reading when he called." The reading was happening, but the call interrupted it. Student: Got it! And if I wanted to show that something happened just before another event in the past, could I use just with Past Perfect? Teacher: Yes, you can! We use just to show that something happened immediately before another action. For example: I had just finished the book when she called. Student: So, "just" gives us the idea that the action was recently completed before something else happened, right? Teacher: Exactly! Let’s review the structure once again: Affirmative: Subject + had + past participle (V3) - Negative: Subject + had + not + past participle (V3) - Question: Had + subject + past participle (V3)? Student: So, to summarize, we use Past Perfect when we want to talk about an action that happened before another action in the past, and we use it with time expressions like before, already, and by the time. Is that right? Teacher: Exactly! And remember, we can also use Past Perfect to explain why something happened in the past. For example: He had studied hard, so he passed the exam. Student: Oh, so Past Perfect helps explain the reason behind past events? Teacher: Yes, exactly! It shows what happened before something else in the past, and it can explain why something happened. Student: Great! Now I feel ready for the quiz. Thanks for the help!~ Concept Quiz ?What is the main purpose of the Past Perfect tense? |To describe an action that happened before another action in the past.| To describe a future action completed before another future action.
